INDIA
HIMACHAL PRADESH APPLE PROCESSING AND MARKETING PROJECT
SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS
i.        This report appraises an apple processing and marketing project
in Himachal Pradesh (HP) for which an IDA Credit of US$13.0 million is
proposed. It would support a four-year lending program for investments
in apple processing and marketing facilities and in related road works,
road maintenance equipment, aerial cableways, technical assistance, training,
and project evaluation.
ii.       Only about 10% of HP's mountainous terrain is cultivated, but 94%
of its 3.5 million people depend on agriculture. Apples are the State's main
cash crop and fastest growing industry, and for many small farmers they have
provided the opportunity to move from subsistence to cash crop farming. IIP
currently accounts for over a quarter of total Indian apple output on land
which is poorly suited to other types of agriculture. Areas increased from
3,000 ha in 1960 to 26,000 ha in 1970, production rose from 10,000 to 76,000
tons, and farmers involved from 7,000 to 30,000. The average grower now
has less than 1 ha of apples which constitutes his sole source of casl
income.
iii.      HP's apple production is expected to reach 250,000 tons and farmers
involved 40,000 to 50,000 by 1978. However, apple processing facilities
and marketing are far from adequate to handle these quantities efficiently:
(a) apple marketing is unregulated; (b) commission agents play a dual role,
often acting as speculative buyers, thus leaving scope for malpractices at
the expense of the growers; (c) there is no uniform g,rading system and
quality of fruit marketed varies widely; (d) apples are seldom cold stored;
(e) only an insignificant part of production is processed; (f) the road
network serving the industry is inadequate for efficient transportation and
warketing.
iv.       The proposed project would introduce technological innovatidots
in processing, a commercially oriented marketing, organization and infrastruc-
ture improvements to handle 55,000 tons  tf apo1es (or 25%' of HP's production
at project completion). It would consist of;
(a)  a network of twelve packing houses, three cdlection
stations, one transshipment center and four -old
storages;
(b)  a juice concentrate plant to utilize cull apples not
marketable as fresh fruit;
(c) 97 km of new road construction, and improvemcnts to about
304 km of existing roads, to provide adequ.:te all-weather



- ii -
road connections between orchard areas and project collection
stations and packing houses, and the necessarY road links
from them to the existing highways system;
(d)  about 40 km of aerial cableways in selected production
areas where the steep terrain precludes economic road
construction;
(e)  training programs and technical assistance; and
(f)  a project evaluation study.
v.        Total project cost is estimated at US$21.7 million, of which the
foreign exchange component is 36%, or US$7.8 million. The proposed IDA
credit of US$13.0 million would finance about 60% of total cost, covering
the whole of project foreign costs pltus 37% of local costs. For processing
and marketing facilities and cableways, funds would be channeled from IDA
through GOI via the Agricultural Refinance Corporation and commercial banks,
to the Horticultural Produce Processing and Marketing Corporation. Funds
allocated for road works, road maintenance equipment, technical assistance,
training and project evaluation would be channeled through GOI to HP
Government.
vi.       To operate the new marketing facilities a Horticultural Produce
Processing and Marketing Corporation (HPMC) would be established as a
subsidiary of the HP Agro-Industries Corporation (AIC) and staffed with
qualified management and operational personnel. HPMC would offer a wide
range of services to growers, inclucling standardized packing, transportation/
forwarding, credit and wholesale selling services at fees lower than the
present alternatives.
vii.      For processing and marketing fac-ilities, HP Public Works Department
(PWD), IIP State Department of Horticulture (SDH) and engineering consultants
would assist IIPMC select sites; draw up tender documents, detailed drawings,
construction and equipment purchase contracts; and evaluate bids for cable-
ways. Most equipment for processing and marketing facilities, all cableways
and some road maintenance equipment would be procured through international
competitive bidding. The buildings for the facilities would be constructed
by local contractors or PWD.  PWD would ilso conduct much of the road works
on force account with manual labor, local contractors would undertake the
balance. This system ihas traditionallv worked well.
viii.     hPMC's annual net profit after project completion should exceed
US$300,000, while its financial rate of return wouild be 14%.
ix.       A Central Project committee would coordinate project implementation.
The committee would be located in SDH which is primarily responsible for
development of the apple industry.  The Committee woasld review annual plans
for project implementation and organise inter-agency cooperation.



- iii -
x.        This would be the first Bank/IDA project in India setting up a
large-scale integrated system aimed at improving processing and marketing
of a perishable horticultural crop.  It would help modernize a significant
sector of the Indian hill areas to the benefit of both producers and
consumers. Significantly higher incomes to some 10,000 fanners, whose
average holding is less than 1 ha, would result from more efficient marketing
of fresh apples, from processing of cull apples and by extending the present
market area and period.  Quantified net economic benefits yield a rate of
return of 23%. The project is suitable for an IDA credit of US$13.0 million.






INDIA
HIMACHAL PRADESH APPLE PROCESSING AND MARKETING PROJECT
I. INTRODUCTION
1.01      The Government of India (GOI) has requested an IDA Credit to meet
part of the costs of providing apple processing facilities and developing
an integrated apple marketing system in the state of Himachal Pradesh (HP).
The project is part of India's program to improve agricultural marketing and
processing activities. The advanced processing technology and new marketing
procedures introduced in the project area would provide a basis for similar
developments of apples and other fruits in other states.
1.02      Total Bank Group lending for Indian agriculture was about US$476
million at June 30, 1973. Until 1967, most of the credits were for irrigation,
but lending has since been broadened to include direct on-farm investments
and ancillary services. In financial year 1973 IDA approved three agricul-
tural projects: two in credit (Madhya Pradesh a.rd Uttar Pradesh) and
one in agricultural wholesale markets (Mysore).
1.03      This appraisal is based on a report from HP Government prepared
with assistance from the FAO/IBRD Cooperative Program in August, 1972, and
on the findings of a mission consisting of Messrs. R. Frank, R. Golkowskv,
D. Lee and F.D.T. Reid (IDA) andtR. Bartram, J. Crafin and G. Mathia
(consultants) that visited India ifneptember/October, 1972. Completion
of this report was held up because data on the road construction and
improvement component of the project could not be finalized until
September, 1973. Financial estimates in this report are at 1972 prices.
II. BACKGROUND
A. 4Wriculture
. 1/~~~~~~A
India-l
2.01      The emphasis of Indian development is now on agriculture, with
priority for the spread of advanced technology through increasing availability
of modern inputs, such as high yielding seed varieties and fertilizer, and
improved water control. Although 70% of India's labor force is in agriculture
(producing two-fifths of GINP), the sector's recent advances have by-passed
large areas of India. There are considerable variations in production each
year, due mainly to weather conditions; the intensity of droughts and floods
significantly affects the yields of the major crops.
1/   A detailed discussion of the present situation and prospects of
agriculture in India is given in Economic Situation and Prospects
of India, Report No. 156-IN, May 8, 1973.



- 2 -
2.02      While irrigation has had a major impact on production, increases
in rainfed areas require further technological advances, together with
institutional and infrastructure improvements. In this context, apple
cultivation is illustrative of the importance of promotino production of high
value crops In rainfed areas. Apple growers in the mountainous states
such as HP are earning acceptable financial returns b- putting into use land
that is poorly suited to other types of agriculture. Continued progress in
this sector requires removal of important bottlenecks in transportation,
processing and marketing.
Himachal Pradesh
2.03      About 96% of HP's 3.5 million people live in rural areas.  Most
of them depend on agriculture for their basic income and provide 60% of
the Gross State Product.? Due to the mountainous terrain, only about  10%
of the State's 56,000 km is cultivated, compared with 45% for the whole
of India. Nearly tL--Dfifths of the State is under permanent pasture. Maize,
wheat and rice ar, cultivated, but the State remains a net importer of
foodgrainE. Fruit, particularly apples, and seed potatoes are the main
cash crops, and are exported to other parts of India. The State has four
main agronomic zones; low hill, mid hill, hi-h hill and dry. The high hill
zone (1,500 to 2,000 m) is the most suitable for temperate fruit production
(Annex 1).
B. Apple Production and Processing
India
2.04      Over the last five years, Indian apple production has increased
on average nbout 30% a year, and is expected to increase at about 17% a year
over the next five years. Farmers use modern production techniques, including
fertilizer application, crop protection and pruning. Standards of orchard
management are generally satisfactory. Extensive mechanizarion is not
feasible owing to the topography. Hence, orchard operations are highly labor-
intensive.
2.05      Of the total 1970/71 production of 273,000 tons of fresh marketed
apples, over a quarter came from HP. liarvest and marketirg seasons of the
important apple supply states overlap for short periods. UP marketing peaks
in Tuly-August, HP in September-October, and Jammu and Kashmir in Ocrober-
November. Only about 1% of production is processed. In 1970, only 17 tons
of jam, 200 tons of juice and 873 tons of preserves were marketed, valued
at about Rs 5 million. Little use is made of culled apples, which can be
as high as 20% in areas such as HP where hail damage is prevalent. The
Indian soft drink industry, which is expanding rapidly, needs a reliable
supply of apple concentrate for processing.



-3-
Himachal Pradesh
2.06      Most expansion of apple production has been in the last decade,
when the advent of roads, coupled with a vast protected Indian market and
reasonable prices, made apple growing attractive on land unsuitable for most
other crops. Between 1960 and 1970, apple areas increased from 3,000 ha to
26,000 ha, production from 10,000 tons to 76,000 tons, and farmers involved
from 7,000 to 30,000. Expansion has been largely in the districts of Simla,
Kulu, Mandi and Sirmur, where the environment is most favorable, and production
was over 90% of the lIP total in 1970/71. Simla is the largest area, with
about half total production. The average grower has slightly less than 1 ha
of apples (Annex 2).
2.07      The most important varieties are Delicious, which were introduced
to HP at the beginning of the century by an American. They are well suited
to HP's agronomic and climatic conditions, and have found strong acceptance
among Indian consumers. Harvesting is in July, August and September, with
the peak in August. The ripe fruits are piclced, and sorted into 3 quality
grades according to their color and degree of skin blemishes. The two top
quality grades are further sorted into 7 standard sizes and packed in
wooden boxes for shipment to urban markets for sale as fresh fruit. The
third grade, normally about 20% of the market, are culls, some of which are
sold for animal food and for processing. Most, however, are disposed of as
waste (Annex 3).
C.  AppLe Marketing
India
2.08      General.  While India produces mangoes, banaras, guava, mandarins,
grapes, litchi and pineapples in significant quantities, apples r'ymain the
most popular frtuit. During the apple season, bananas and mandarins are
also on the market, but they are not usually regarded as a sul;stitute for
apples by consumers.
2.09      India has neither exported nor imported apples commercially in recent
years. The farm-gate cost of production, currently about Rs 7S5/ton hefore
packing, is in line with prevailing world prices. However, early prospects
for large-scale export are not good. The prerequisites for such exports are
not in sight, particularly packing materials industries, refrigerated
trucking services, waterfront cold storage and a competent trade organization
for international marketing.
2.10      Almost all marketed apples are currently consumed in Bombay, Calcutta,
Delhi, Madras, Hyderabad and Bangalore, in order of importance.  A further
72 urban markets with populations of 100,000 or more remain largely untapped.
2.11      Present Marketing Arrangements.  Most production is sold on con-
signment by commission agents on behalf of growers, immediately on arrival



- 4 -
at wholesale auction markets. Consignments are usually bought by sub-
wholesalers who sell direct to retailers. In theory, the commission agents
sell for a fixed commission of 7%, considered exorbitant by growers. In
practice, many of them act as sub-wholesalers and/or speculative buyers as
well, giving scope for widespread malpractices at the expense of the
consignors. Agents frequently pay growers after unnecessarily long delays.
2.12      Although the Agricultural Produce (Grading and Marketing) Act, 1937,
prescribes procedures and standards for grading and marketing apples and
other produce, apple marketing is not in practice regulated, since the
law is not enforced.
2.13      In 1970/71 only about 12,000 tons of apples (8% of the marketed
crop) was placed in cold storage for off-season marketing. This is in-
sufficient to extend the marketing season significantly. The available
storage is primarily for potatoes, and cannot provide the lower temperatures
and more exacting humidity controls required for apples. By building cold
storage facilities specifically for apples, their marketing period could be
considerablv extended.
2.14      Prices.  The general wholesale price level has lately remained
fairly high at an average of about Rs 2.00/kg, albeit fluctuating widely
from a low of Rs 0.81 in November, 1971, to a high of Rs 5.55 in March, 1972.
On average, prices have tended to decline in recent years as increased
production has been marketed. Indian consumption in 197n/71, about 270,000
tons, is only about one-half kg per capita, compared witlh 10-30 kg in
developed countries. Sufficient market potential exists to absorb increased
production over the next decade or so, but unless appropriate measures are
taken to improve marketing methods and facilities, the seasonal gluts will
intensify, and prices suffer.
Ilimachal Pradesh
2.15      Present Marketing Problems.  Farmers have expandod plantings into
new and more remote areas faster thlan feeder roads have been built.
Transportation is more and more difficult and costly in these rerot" areas,
where porterage was often the onlv method!. Iowever, labor willing to
undertake porterage is becoming scarce.  The packed fruit is leFt by tlhc
roadside for collection, usually withrut adequate weather protection,
causing unnecessary deterioration.  Even where roads are available they
are often of inferior standard, with consequmnt frequent blockages due
to rain and lindslides in the monsoon, when apnles are sent to market.
2.16      The numerous commission agents often send fairly small shipments
to markets. Delhi is 350 km from Simla, the State capital, Calcutta 1,800
km and Bombav 1,800 km. Since most State roads are winding and narrow, and
because of interstate transport regulations, consignments are usually tranship-
ped en route. During the fresh app]e marketing months, the temperatture is
usually very high, leading to spoilage during stops and transfers. Estimated
average transportation cost per ease of 18 kg net from the producing areas



-5-
is about Rs 5.35 to Delhi, Rs 9.90 to Calcutta and Rs 10.55 to Bombay, in-
cluding municipal tax, forwarding, loading and unloading charges. Spoilage
and losses can be up to 20% (Annex 4).
2.17      Apart from a few simple mechanical graders no'7 being used by some
leading growers, all grading is by hand. Individual farmers grade and pack
their own produce without any standardized quality control. Quality thus
varies widely from grower to grower and from box to box. This is a serious
hindrance to rational marketing, as prospective buyers are forced to inspect
many boxes from each lot on offer. This iF time-consuming and contributes
to market congestion. It frequently compels wholesalers to regrade and
repack before resale.
2.18      Need for the Project.  The three major marketing cost components
are: (i) packing; (ii) transportation; and (iii) commission. There is
much scope for improving all three, as well as decreasing transit losses,
and making better use of culls (presently largely wasted). All such improve-
ments would result in increased income to growers and/or lower consumer
prices.
2.19      Cost of transportation of HP apples to markets other than Delhi
represents around 45 to 55% of wholesale price. This could be reduced to
around 25% through a coordinated marketing effort. Transport time to Bombay
or Calcutta could be reduced from the present 20 to 30 days bv from 7 to 15
days with careful plhasing of transport. This would reduce deterioration in
transit and increase shelf life.
2.20      Upgrading existing roads in production areas to all-weather
standards would decrease blockages and wash-outs, and would decrease apple
spoilage. New roads are required in some of tihe niew planted areas, and where
the terrain is too steep, cableways can transport apples and other goods at
much less cost.
2.21      While good quality packing materials aro available, and are utsed
consistently by the leading growers, they are far too eypensive for the
average grower. Mony are financially unable to use such high quality materials
in spite of their obvious profitability. The cost of such material is
expected to increase further.  Research and devcl)iment in alternative designs
and standardization are requirod to reduce cost.  A large-scale integrated
packing and marketing operation is an ideal vehicle for such innovations.
2.22      The introduction of quality control in shipments from tLhe smaller
growers would give them greater incomes.  The lar,er 2rowers have demonstrated
that lower selling costs and Tvigher wholesale prires can be obtained for
well-packed app?es of consistent quality.
2.23      The formation of a commercially oriented marketin,- organization
with advanced and integrated marketing facilities, would offer growers an
alternative marketing channel, and provide competition for commission agents,
thereby increasing marketing efficiency and stimulating the introduction
of new types of marketing arrangements and facilities.



- 6 -
D. Institutions
State Department of Horticulture (SDH)
2.24      SDH is the main government agency concerneci with the apple industry.
It employs almost 800 staff, of which more than 700 are in the field. Through
two Deputy Directors (Horticultural Development and liorticultural Extension)
it promotes horticulture throughout HP.  It has more than 70 progeny-cum-demon-
stration orchards and stations, and has test- many kinds and varieties of
fruits. SDH has taken a lead in improving marketing and introducing proces-
sing of apples.  It has stimulated the establishment of a transhipment center
in Parwanu, cold storage units in Delhi and Bombay, and wholesale shops in
the Delhi market.  It has recently negotiated freight rates with the trucking
industry and organized the supply of packing cases and production inputs.
It also operates a chain of small pilot fruit canning units.
Public 1Works Depar:-nent (PWD)
2.25      HP's P11D is organized much as in other states, and has played an
important role in developing the apple industry by implementing an extensive
road construction program over the last two decades. However, shortage of
funds and labor problems in the remote areas lhave prevented the program from
keeping pace with the increase in traffic resulting from the rapid expansion
of apple production. Wiithin the above limitations its standard of
road construction has been good.
Agro-Industries Corporation of Hirachal Pradeslh (AIC)
2.26      Following GOI encouragement of agro-industries, PP Governnment
established AIC in 1970.  Of its authorized share capital of Rs 20 million
(HIP Government 51%, GOI 49%), Rs 14 million had been pail up by the end
of fiscal 1973. The Director of Horticulture is one of the five Board
members.  Until recently AIC's main activities were the procurement and
distribution of tractors and some minor agricultural implements. It h1as
also handled and forwarded apples from the Parwanu warehouse (rented from
SDII) to major markets.  In the 1972 season it conisigned apples to MAFCO
(a retailing organization in Bom,bav) on behalf of growers.  In its first
full operatinp year (ending March 31, 1972) it madp a profit after tax of
Rs 118,00)(.   Financial planning is weak, and1 ATC's management has recruited
an experienced accountant to improve it.
III. T6'l PT'tJFCT
A. Definition
3.0!1     The project would establishi new market outlets for about one
quarter 1'P'q estimated 1978 production by providing alternative, more
integrated and more efficient processing an(l marketing services.  These



-7-
would improve producer prices, double the present marketing period, reduce
growers' dependence on existing wholesaler/commission agents, and utilize
some currentlv wasted culls. The project would help prevent the seasonal
apple gluts anticinated within the next 5 to 10 years because of rapidly
increasing production, and lagging processing and mnarketing facilities.
The financial rate of return on apple growing (24% on a 1 ha orchard -
Annex 2) is extremely attractive, and almost all. returns are cash. The
annual net revenue on a 1 ha orchard is about Rs 7,iOr), coirpared with about
Rs 2,)00 for most seasonal crops in HJP. The project would handle 55,000
tons of apples and consist of:
(a) a network of twelve packing houses, three collection
stations, one transshipment center and four cold storages;
(b)  a juice concentrate plant to utilize 11,000 tons of
apples not marketable as fresh fruit;
(c)  road construiction (97 Km) and improvements (34)4 Kum)
providing (i) sufficient all-weather road connections
between orchard areas and project collection stations
and packing lhouses, and (ii) the necessary road links
from them to the existing highways svstemr
(d) aerial cableways in selected production areas where the
steep terrain precludes economic road construction;
(e)  training programs and cechnical assistance: and
(f)  a project evaluation study.    (l
Project size is based on the assumption thiat about 40(% of growers in the
project supply areas would wish to use project facilities, with the remainder
using existing facilities.
B.  Detailed Features-
Packine Pouses
3.02       A total of 12 packing houses would be constructed in HtP's four
maior apple production districts  (Simla. Kulu, l4andi and Sirmur  see para
2.06 and Annexes 2 and 5) over a four-vear period. Within tihese four
districts, the 12 packing houses and supporting 3 collection stations (see
para 3.04) would he located in the areas of highest Production density.
Production in the potential packina houses supplv areas would total about
138,000 tons at oroject comnpletion in 1977.  The HP authorities have agreed
upon tentative locations and capacities for the packing houses and collec-
1/   For further details see Annex 5 and Charts WB-7264, WB-7265, WB-7266,
WB-7267, WB-7288(R), WB-7320(4R), WB-7321(3R).



- 8 -
tion stations (Annex 5, Table 1). The annual capacity of thie individual
packing houses would be between 3,000 and 5,000 tons based on three months'
usage and two work shifts during the peak harvest months. Number, locations
and capacities of the proposed packing house and collection station network
were based on the conservative assumption that in the areas served by the
network about 40% of the growers would use the project facilities for their
production.
3.03      The packing houses would receive loosely packed apples from the
orchards, and grade and pack themn for shipment to consumers. During the
grading, culls would be segregated for shipment to a processing plant. The
packing houses would be used during the non-harvest season for storage and
distribution of farm inputs. Each packing house would be incorporated in
a single span building (20 m x 75 m) with an insulated roof and a flat
concrete floor. The building would provide three working areas: (a)
reception and pregrading storage; (b) grading and packing; and (c) premarket
storage and dispatch.  Two variable speed grading lines would be installed
parallel to the length of the building. The grading equipment would consist
of feeder belts, sizers, sorting tables and packing tables.  The proposed
configuration would provide for mechanical size grading and manual quality
grading and packing. The packing houses would also be equipped with stands,
generators, hand trolleys, wooden pallets, packing stands and office equipment
and furniture.
Collection Stations
3.04      Three apple collection stations would be built to help supply
several Dacking houses in areas of moderate concentration of production
where packing lhouses would not be presentlv justified.  Thieir fuinction
would be to provide a weather-protected point for onward transport to the
nearest packing lhouse.  During the non--harvest period they would also be
used for the storage and distribution of farm inouts.  The building design
would be the same as for packing houses so that when local production
increased, the necessary equipment could be installed to convert them into
packing houses.  The collection stations would be equipped with wooden
pallets, hand trolleys and office furniture ancd equipment.
Transshipment Center
3.05      The project would include a transshipm-.ient center wliiclh would be
constructed at the Faryana/Delhi border.  This wouldc attract a siubstantial
proportion of the produce which now passes through tihe intermediate D)elhi
market for ultimate disposal in Boribay, Calcutta an'd distanL markets.
This proportion has been estimated at about 2(),Of)O tons, and would rc-quire
an installed storage capacity of 60ri tons in the new transshiipment center.
A simple shelter to protect the apples from direct sunlight and rain and
with easy access for the simultaneous loading and unloading of a number of
trucks would suffice. The whole storage area and central roadway would have
a well-drained concrete base, electric lighting and water supply. A series
of standpines and noses would allow the concrete floor and surroundings to be
hosed down to assist cooling in extreme heat.



-9-
Cold Storage Facilities
3.06      Four cold storage units of 2,0n0  tons capacity each, would be
built in two of the major apple markets, thiree in New Delhi and one in
Calcutta.  The facilities would be utilized for short-term storage during
the August and September harvest period. In late September they would be
filled with apples for storage and eventual off-season marketing in January
through March.  From April to August the units would be used to store other
products, such as potatoes and spices. Each unit would consist of a fiber-
glass insulated five story 100 ft x 110 ft building partitioned into five
multistory compartments, each of 400 ton capacity. An ammonia-based
refrigeration system would be installed to provide a temperature range of
-100C to 7°C. The air ducts would provide proper circulation and evaporators
would insure relative humidity levels of at least 90%. Elevators would move
apples between floors (paras 3.20 and 4.09).
Processing Plant
3.07      An apDle juice concentrate plant would be built to process apples
which cannot be sold as fresh fruit (paras 3.11 and 4.09).  The plant would
have an annual capacitv of 12,000 tons, based on about a three month
operation. During the off-season the plant wouild be used to process otther
types of fruits grown in the area such as peaches, plums and pears. The
basic processing facility would be designed to prodtuce apple juice concentrate
(1:6) for transport in bulk by insulate(I tank trucks to major consumer areas
where it would be reconstituted and bottled bv soft drink mantufacturers and
distributors.  The proposed plant woul(d include facilities for washing and
processing; aroma recovery and restoration: juice concentration, pasteurization
and clarification. It would also include a small processing laboratory and
bottling installation to enable the plant to supply the local market with
bottled juice.
Road Works and Maintenance Equipment
3.08      The project provides for an extensive program for inmproving exist-
ing roads and constructing new roads to ensure appropriate and timely transport
of apples to packing houses and packled apples to markets.  As apples are
harvested, processed and transported duiring the monsoon seasoni, Ltme roads
would be bitumen surfaced to resist danage due to weather and to truck transit.
The roads would fall into two standard categories: (a) access, built to a
formation width of 3.65 ni with ;m 2.45 rn widc bitumen suirface, and (h) feeder,
built to a formation width of 4.9 rn with a 2.45 m wide bitumen sLarface.  About
14 km of access and 83 km of feeder roads would be newly constructed, whlile
304 kn of feeder roads would be unv'raded by improvingv cross drainag,e, foundations
and surfacing.  To reduce landsl iie-caused transport delavs, whiich can be as
long as a week, the project wouild provide PWD with additional inainten;ance
equipment.  For further details see paras 4.05, 4.11 and 4.13 and Annex 6.



- 10 -
Aerial Cableways
3.09      A number of aerial cableways of a total length of about 40 kmii would
be installed for collection of apples from orchards on steep slopes.  Each
cableway installation would consist of trestles supporting a continuous cable
at a height of about 3 m, and driven by an electric motor. The cable would
allow boxes or baskets of apples to be suspended for transport directly to
a nearby collection station, packing lhouse or roadside. Farmers oln slopes
served by cableways would carry their Drodu-P via several horizontal paths
leading to loading stations along the cableways. For details on cableways
procurement and operation see paras 3.21, 4.06 and 4.12.
Technical Assistance, Training and Project Evaluation
3.10      Technical assistance would be nrovided and spread over thie four-
year proiect period (para 3.12) to insure proper project implementation and
training of personnel. An experienced packing plant manager would be engaged
for 9 months (split into three assignn'ents over two years) and an apple
quality control expert for 12 months to conduct .-raining courses and provide
in-service training for future packing plant managers, nacking line
superintendents, grade inspectors and cold storage managers. The experts
would participate in preparing bidding documents for collection stations,
packing houses and the transshipment center, and also organize overseas
training for project staff.  A senior marketing specialist (two three-months
assignments over two years) would train marketing staff and organize
overseas training for 3 sales office managers. He would also supervise a
study to be conducted on the market prospects of apple products, particularly
juice.  To train personnel for the juice concentrate plant and to assist in
initial operations of this plant, a food technologist would for 18 months
(four assignments over four years) and a food processing microbiologist
would for one vear (two assignments over three years) work tinder hP Govern-
ment contracts.
3.11      For the juice plant, a food processing engineering firm. would be
engaged to (a) orovide design specifications and layouts; (b) assist in
bidding procedures and award analysis, and (c) assist in construction
supervision. For the cold storage facilities, an engineering consultant
firm would be engaged to (a) provide the design and specification for thie
units; (b) assist in bidding procedures and award analysis; (c) assist in
construction supervision. For further details on technical assistanci: and
training, see para 4.16 and Annex 7. Details on thie project evaluation
study are in para 4.17.
C.  Engineering and Construction Schedule
3.12      Planning and construction of the processing and marketing, facili-
ties would be phased over three and a half years to coincide withi the increase
in prodtiction in specific locations and the development of management and
operational personnel.  The road imtrovement and construction program wvould

facilities are outlined in Annex 8, and World Bank Chart 7320(4R) gives a

F. Procurement
3.19      Construction of buildings for collection stations, packing houses,
cold storage units, the transshipment center, the juice plant, and the road
improvement and construction works wouldl be phased over four years (para
3.12). Civil works would amount to about US$72,000 for a typical collection
station and packing house, about US$83,000 for the transshipment center and
about US$80,000 for the juice plant. These facilities would be widely
distributed geographically. Bulking would not be practical, and as such
contracts would not be attractive to expatriate firms international
competitive bidding would not be suitable.  Local contractors are capable
of carrying out these civil works, which would be put out to local tender
bv HPNC.
3.20      Grading equipment for each packing house would cost from UlS$30,000
to 35,000 and contracts would be awarded for not less than two packing
houses. Equipment for the juice concentrate plant wotuld cost more than
US$600,000 and refrigeration equipment for each of the four cold storage
units about ITS$100,000.  All this essential equinment would be procured
by HIPYC as intearated operating svstenis through international competitive
bidding following Bank Group Guidelines for Procurement.  Minor equipment
items which cannot be grouped in pacl<as,es of USS5O,5000 or more would be
procured through local competitive bliddinr'.
3.21      The minimum length of a cableway would be 2 km costing about
US$100,000, but most cableways would be 5 krll or longer and cost about
US$250,000. Knowledge of light aerial cableways as proposed under tie
project is limited in India.  Followin>' Bank Group international conmpetitive
bidding procedures, .PMIC would invite bidders to present their respective
engineering studies and design proposals. Awards shouild be given for
turnkey bids, and contracts would be grouped as much as feasible. Of the
road maintenance equipment, bulldozers would be procured through interna-
tional competitive bidding bv PIJT).

3.22      Due to a relatively short construction season because of the
weather and the consequent difficulty in attracting contractors, the State
executes about 70% of its road development by force account, with local
contractors undertaking the balance. Accordingly, this method would be
used for project road works. At negotiations, a Program was agreed, covering
road construction and improvement related to the programs for establishing
collection stations and packing houses (Annex 6, Appendix 1).  Road main-
tenance equipment, for which bulking would not be practical would be put to
contract under local bidding.
3.23      Bidding documents, including specifications for goods procured
through international competitve bidding, would be sent to IDA for review
and comment before issue. IDA would also comment on the evaluation of bids
and recommendations for awards. For works or goods procured through local
competitive bidding, a summary of bids or quotations, an analysis report
and a brief justification of the contract award would be sent to IDA at the
time the award was made.

IV. ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. Institutions
Horticultural Produce Processing and Marketing Corporation
4.01      The advanced and highly integrated project processing and
marketing activities would require specialized management personnel and
appropriate operating procedures. A specialized corporation, the Horti-
cultural Produce Processing and Marketing Corporation (HPMC), would be
established as an AIC subsidiary and staffed with qualified management
and operational personnel. The outline of its financial and operational
structure is in Annex 11. With an equity of Rs 20 million which would be
HPMC's permanent working capital and project loans amounting to about Rs 50
million (including physical contingencies) which would finance HPMC's
initial fixed assets, HPMC's debt/equity ration would be about 2.5, and this
is acceptable. The proposed legal structure would allow HPMC to: (a) engage
in commercial buying, processing and selling of horticultural produce;
(b) lend or advance money to growers as part of its procurement activities-
(c) provide short-term suppliers' credit to entities marketing the products
and (d) pay competitive and incentive-based salaries to key managers and
employees. GOI and UP Government officials have taken steps to form HPIIC
and at negotiations IDA and the Indian delegation agreed on the Articles
of Association of HPMC and the qualifications and experience of HPIIC's
managing director. HPTMC's formation, in a manner satisfactory to IDA, would
be a condition of effectiveness.
4.02      The proposed organizational structure of HPMC provides for a
Board of Directors, Managing Director and three major operating departments:
Purchasing and Packing, Processing, Storage and Marketing, as well as an
Administrative and Accounting department. The Purchasing and Packing
Department would have field staff throughout the four project districts to
arrange purchases or marketing service agreements with growers, as well as
staff to manage and operate the collection and packing stations. The
Storage and Marketing Department would staff the cold storage facilities
in New Delhi and Calcutta. The existing AIC cold storage facility in Bombay
would be taken over by HPMC at a valuation to be agreed between them, and
endorsed by ARC. HPMC would have sales representatives in some ten other
urban markets. At full development it would employ about 200 full time, mainly
managerial, operational, clerical and service staff.  In addition HPMC and
AIC (related to cableways) would employ at least 2,000 temporary workers for
about three months during the peak harvesting and marketing season.
4.03      IPMC would offer a wide range of services to growers.  First, a
standardized packing and transportation/forwarding service on a fee basis
to growers who wished to continue having their produce marketed by the
commission agents in the wholesale markets. Second, for growers who wished
to have their apples marketed during the harvest season it would provide
standardized packing, forwarding and wholesale selling services at fees



- 16 -
lower than the present alternatives. Third, it would be prepared to
purchase apples directly from growers for a fixed price. After grading
and packing they would be stored in HPMC's cold storage facilities for
off season marketing. Finally, it would purchase up to 11,000 tons/year
of culls from growers, and this would increase the average growers sales
revenue by 5%.
State Department of Horticulture
4.04      SDH would provide supplementary marketing and processing services
so as to produce the maximum benefits to the industry. In particular, it
would establish a grading inspection service to standardize apple size and
quality grading of the State's apple production. HP Government gave
assurances at negotiations that grading inspectors would be stationed at
HPMC's packing houses (and at any other individual packing operation which
wanted the benefit of the grading inspection), and that SDH's certifying
grade would be marked on all approved cases for subsequent sale. In
addition, SDH would have representatives in the major markets to resolve
claims arising from grading disputes between buyers and sellers. To
assist in establishing the grading inspection service, SDH would engage
a quality control expert (para 3.10) to define standards, train inspectors,
and establish a fruit cold storage research program to be carried out
jointly by SDH and the University of HP.
Public Works Department
4.05      PWD would carry out the road works, which would form part of the
State's Road Development Plan for the next four years. PWD would also assist
(a) HPMC in planning and supervising the installation of the cableways, and
(b) AIC in operating and maintaining them, in particular by providing cable-
way operators (para 4.06). In addition, PWD would operate project road
maintenance equipment, which would be strategically placed in the four
project districts so as to deal rapidly with road blockages due to landslides,
washouts and snow storms.
Agro-Industries Corporation
4.06      AIC's role in project implementation would be limited to operating
the cableways, which would be rented to them by HPMC. Rental fees would be
determined on a break-even basis. Since the cableways would be used for
the transportation of other goods outside the apple harvest season, includ-
ing other agricultural produce, fertilizers and consumer goods, it would be
more appropriate for AIC to operate them on a year-round basis, rather than
ifPMC, whose major interest would be with the apple harvest. Cableways would
be a new means of transportation for goods in this part of India, but there
is no reason to doubt that in the project areas of HP they would become as
popular as in other mountainous parts of the world.



- 17 -
Agricultural Refinance Corporation -/
4.07      ARC is a subsidiary of the Reserve Bank of India and is well
known to IDA as a lending channel for a variety of IDA supported projects
including the Bihar and Mysore Agricultural Markets Projects and a number
of agricultural credit projects. Its general function is to refinance
agricultural or agriculturally-related investment schemes taken up by Land
Development Banks, the State Cooperative Banks, and commercial banks. ARC's
professional staff of over 100 includes a small technical group and staff
at 14 regional offices. ARC is competently managed and in sound financial
condition. Its role is important, not only as a channel for development
funds, but also for its influence on the lending policies and procedures of
the agencies it assists. It is a suitable institution for supervising
implementation of key project components.
Central Project Committee
4.08      A Central Project Committee would be established to coordinate
inter-agency cooperation as necessary for project implementation. For
administrative purposes it would be located in SDH which would provide the
necessary secretarial services. Committee membership would include the
Agricultural Production Commissioner, the Secretaries of Finance and Transpor-
tation, representatives of ARC, PWD and SDH, the Managing Directors of AIC
and HPMC, a representative of apple growers and two representatives of GOI.
The Committee would review and propose annual plans to State Government
and HPMC for road works and cableway construction; packing and collection
station construction; cold storage and transshipment center construction;
purchase of equipment; and training, to ensure that all these interrelated
activities were properly integrated. Assurances were obtained at negotia-
tions that the Committee would be formed with responsibilities as above,
and it would be a condition of effectiveness that the Committee had been
set up and was operating.
B. Engineering, Contracting and Supervision
Processing and Marketing Facilities

the processing and marketing facilities. HPMC would hire engineering
consultants to conduct site investigations and prepare alternative plans,
designs and tender documents for the cold stores and the juice concentrate
plant. The consultants would also assist HPMC to evaluate bids and quper-
vise construction items, equipment installation and test runs of the cold

C. Project Accounts and Audit
4.14      At negotiations assurances were obtained from ARC that it would,
and from AIC that it would cause HPMC to (a) have its accounts and financial
statements for each fiscal year audited, in accordance witil sound auditing
principles consistently applied, by independent auditors acceptable to IDA;
(b) furnish to IDA not later than four months after the end of each year,
(i) certified copies of its financial statements for such year as so audited
and (ii) the auditors report; and (c) furnish to IDA such other information
concerning the accounts and financial statements of ARC and HPIC and the
audit thereof as IDA would reasonably request. ARC gave also assurances at
negotiations that it would (a) require the PCBs to maintain separate accounts
for project lending; and (b) ensure that the annual audited accounts of such



- 19 -
PCBs would be submitted to IDA within four months of the end of their fiscal
year together with a statement of project lending for such PCBs, certified
by ARC.
4.15      hP Government gave assurances at negotiations that (a) PWD and SDH
would maintain records adequate to record the progress of the project (in-
cluding the cost thereof) and to identify the goods and services financed
out of the proceeds of the IDA Credit, and to disclose the use thereof in
the project; (b) it would enable IDA's representatives to inspect the
Project, the goods financed out of such proceeds and any relevant records
and documents; and (c) it would furnish to IDA all such information as IDA
would reasonably request concerning the project, the expenditure of the
proceeds of the credit and the goods and services financed out of such
proceeds.
D. Training and Project Evaluation Study
Training
4.16      Training in India and abroad would be given to both senior and
junior project staff. Senior staff would be trained mainly individually,
while most junior staff would be trained in groups of more than 10. Local
training would consist of two major components: instruction courses with
occasional field trips, and on-job training. Three types of training
courses (and attendance at them) would be financed under the project:
(a) quality control; (b) packing plant, cold storage and sales management;
and (c) sales promotion. All these courses would be held in India, some of
them probably in HP. On-job training would be given during both the
construction and initial operation periods of the project facilities. The
consultants would play a key role in planning and implementing the
training programs (paras 3.10 and 3.11). It would be a condition of
effectiveness that SDH had under contract a quality control expert, a senior
packing plant manager and a senior marketing specialist, and that their
qualifications were satisfactory to IDA. PlWD would have trained a sufficient
number of cableway technicians.
Project Evaluation Study
4.17      This would be the first Bank/IDA project in India setting up a
large-scale integrated system aimed at improving processing and marketing of
a perishable horticultural crop.  At negotiations, assurances were obtained
from HP Government that by July 1, 1974, it would take all reasonable steps
to enter into a contract satisfactory to IDA with an appropriate ecolnomic
research institution or university to evaluate thle impact of the project.
Details of the proposed contract and terms of reference are in Annex 12.

V. PRODUCTION, MARKETS, PRICES AND FINANCIAL RESULTS
A. Production
5.01      Production in the project area and in HP as a whole has been
projected on the basis of SDH records of plantings and yields. Thus, by
1977, production in HP will be at least 233,000 tons (compared with 122,000
tons in 1972) and in the areas served by the packing houses 138,000 tons.
Additional plantings and improved orchard practices might increase yields
further. The proposed total capacity of the packing houses (55,000 tons)
represents on the average 40% of present production in the areas served
by the packing houses (paras 3.01 and 3.02 and Annex 5).
B. Marketing
5.02      HPMIC would provide packing and forwarding services for 40,000 net
tons of apples (55,000 tons gross before grading) by 1977. Of this total,
20,000 tons would be handled by HPMC on a fee basis for growers who choose
to continue selling apples through commission agents on consignment. Another
10,000 tons would be packed, forwarded and marketed on a commission basis.
A total of 10,000 tons would be purchased by HPMC and, after packing and
forwarding, transported to its cold storage units in Calcutta, Bombay and
New Delhi. Although most of HPMC's output would reach consumers in these
three cities in increasing amounts, HPYC would endeavor to develop orderly
apple marketing in some of the other 75 cities with populations of 100,000
or more (para 2.10 and Annex 11).
C.  Prices and Financial Results
5.03      HPMC's packing operations would begin showing financial profit
from 1978 onward.  In the initial three years, to attract sufficient volume
for its packing houses, HPIHC would offer concessionary rates to growers
interested only in utilizing the packing and forwarding services. The
initial rate to them would be Rs 4.00 per 18 kg case, significantly below
their present packing costs (Rs 5.17/case for materials only). HPMC's
packing fee would be increased to Rs 7.00 per case by 1978 and at this
level the operation would yield a net operating income of Rs 400,000
annually.
5.04      The forwarding service provided by HPMC would consist of
transportation from the packing houses to the transshipment points, ship-
ment and transportation to the urban markets. The forwarding operation
1/   This section based on 1972 prices (para 1.03).



- 21 -
would be basically an infrastructure activity serving the industry by
achieving lower transport and transfer charges through collective, large
volume arrangements. Depending on the destination market, the transport
cost reductions would range from 6% to 30%. After modest losses during
the first two years, HPMfC's forwarding operation would begin showing a
small profit by 1977.
5.05      The charge for the collective marketing service which IHPMC would
provide for growers wishing to sell their apples fresh during the harvest
season would be 5% of the wholesale price which HPYC obtained in the
markets. Commission agents currently charge individual growers a 7% fee
for the same services, including preharvest advances. The reduction is
possible because of the larger scale of operation. On a volume of
10,000 tons the annual net operating profit would be about Rs 700,000.
5.06      1TWprc'  trading of 6,000 tons of cold stored apples would produce
a net operating profit of Rs 1.4 million in 1975. By 1976 and onward, HPMC
would be marketing 10,000 tons for a net annual profit of Rs 2.3 million,
after deducting Rs 6.00 per case for storage costs.  HPMC's operations of
the cold storage facilities would derive revenues both from cold storing
apples and other products as a service. The net annual profit from this
service from 1977 and onward would be about Rs 1.0 million.
5.07      HlPMC's, juice plant is expected to he in full operation by 1978,
when iL would be processing 11,000 tons of culls, equivalent to 20% of all
apples handled through the packing houses. The basic product of the plant
would be 1,100 tons of juice concentrate. Although potential customers,
soft drink bottlers and distributors, indicated a willingness to pay
Rs 10/kg for such concentrate, for purposes of judging financial viabilitv,
it has been valued at Rs 8/kg. At this price, and paying Rs 0.25/kg to
growers for culls which are currently disposed of as waste, the plant would
earn a net operating profit of Rs 3)0,')DO from 1478 onward.
5.08      At full project development, IIPI'C's operating profit (before
interest, tax and depreciation) would amount to Rs 13.4 million per annum.
Net profit (after interest, tax and (lepreciation) would be Rs 2.5 million
during H-IPIIC's second full operatiotn year and increase thereafter by about
Rs 100,000 Der annum.  The financial rate of return would he 14%. (Annex 13).
Any surpluses left after payments of reasonable dividendls to AIC would he
used for the expansion of IIPMC's facilities, particularlv processing,
handling and storage.
VI. BENEEFITS ANT JUSTIFICATION
6.01      At full development, the project would provide an integrated
processing and marketing systein for abotut 55,000 tons of apples annually,
which would he about one-fourth of Lotal IIP production in 1977. As apples
are the state's fastest growing industry and the main cash crop of HP farmers,
the project would meet one of lIP's highest ranking investment needs.  The



- 22 -
improved marketing of HP apples would result in higher producer incomes
through more efficient marketing of fresh fruit, processing of culls
and increasing the present market area and period (Annex 14).
6.02      A major economic contribution of the project would be a reduction
in the seasonal apple gluts which are developing as a result of the very
rapid increase in Indian apple production. This would be achieved by:
(a) taking 10,000 tons off the fresh apple market antd into cold storage
for off-season marketing; (b) extending the fresh apple marketing season
forward by early deliveries through coordinated forwarding operations and
streamlined sales procedures, thereby skipping the time-consuming wholesale
operations in Delhi and other intermediate centers; and (c) developing new
markets in hitherto-neglected urban centers. Apples handled by HPMC would
command premiums which have been calculated conservatively as follows (whole-
sale level): (a) 5% for the 20,000 tons packed and graded for a fee; (b) 13%
for 10,000 tons marketed on consignment; and (c) 37% for 10,000 tons
purchased for cold storage.
6.03      The project would also substantially reduce the transport costs
of apples from orchard to market, by constructing cableways and roads to
reduce the costs of getting apples to the existing road network, by improving
the standard and the maintenance of existing roads, and by transporting
apples directly on long hauls to distant markets instead of having them go
first to the Delhi market. Orchard to roadhead transport costs would be
one-third lower with the project than without it. Roadhead to market costs
would be reduced by about one-fourth. Furthermore, the roads, cableways
and road maintenance equipment would save an estimated 5% of the project
areas' total apple crop from rotting caused by truck breakdowns, or road
blockages due to landslides.  The infrastructure improvements would also
reduce transport costs for other goods in the area and would stimulate new
production there.
6.04      Finally, the juice concentrate plant would utilize otherwise-
wasted cull apples, thereby increasing farmers' incomes while still being
quite profitable to HPMC (para 5.07).
6.05      To calculate the economic return of the project the opportunity
cost of foreign exchange has been valued at Rs 10 per US$1.1/ Economic costs
would include investment and replacement costs for fixed assets (net of
import duties and taxes), operating costs of HPMC (net of payments to
farmers for their apples and intra-HPMC transfer payments), maintenance costs
of PWD, and HPMC permanent working capital requirements.
1/   In rough terms, the opportunity cost of foreign exchange is the increase
in the Rupee value of consumption that results from each $1.00 of
imports. Since India's import duties-to-imports ratio is 30-35%, the
average $1.00 of imports increases consumption by Rs 7.5 multiplied by
1.3-1.35, or by about Rs 10. Other methodologies for estimating India's
opportunity cost of foreign exchange have generally produced values in
the same range.



- 23 -
6.06      At full project development, annual benefits would amount to about
Rs 68.2 million and average annual cost to about Rs 36.1 million. Net
benefits would consist of HPMC surpluses (Rs 44.1 million revenues minus
Rs 32.9 million costs equal Rs 11.2 million), income increments for growers
using_HPMC services (Rs 14.4 miliin), income increments for other growers
in the project supply areas (4.2 million), development benefits accruing
in the project supply area (Rs 1.6 million), and other road users' savings
(Rs 3.9 million), minus the increase in road maintenance costs (Rs 3.2 million).
Benefits have been estimated on the basis of domestic apple and apple products
prices (which are 20% to 30% lower than landed costs of such products if
thev could be imported).
6.07      Based on the above assumptions the rate of return to the economy
would be 23%. A significant result of sensitivitv tests is that the economic
rate of return would drop or rise by about 1% per 27 increase or decrease in
total costs. While it appears unlikely that the economic viability of the
project would be endangered, the high sensitivity to cost increases means
that the HPMC management must be highly competent and cost-conscious.
6.08      Most of the project benefits would accrue to some 10,000 project-
area farmers, whose average holding is less than 1 hectare. They would also
gain from reductions in the market power of the large traders. The main
nonquantifiable project benefits would be the development of a new marketing
institution and the social advantages of an expanded transport system.
Together, they should have a far reaching and favorable impact on the
economv and the societv of a neglected region in India.
Ecology
6.09      The project would help reduce wastage of culls that growers would
otherwise continue to shed into the orchards. With the project, the only
ecological problem which is likely to arise would be the disposal of
about 1,000 tons of dry pomace (Annex 5), the major bv-nroduct of the ,juice
concentrate plant. Since the exnected price for juice concentrate would
cover all cost and a reasonable profit (para 5.07), the pomace could be
sold at a fairly modest price as cattle feed.

1.         About 96% of tiP's 3.5 million people live in rural areas and 94Z
depend on agriculture/horticulture.  however, a good deal of the farming
population pursues some subsidiary occupation, suclh as lumbering, roadwork
and weaving to supplement the income from land. Due to the mountainous
topography--ltP covers mtuch of the Indian part of the himalayas--only about
10% of the total geograpihical area of 55,660 km2 is cultivated (Table 1).
tiP remainls a net imaporter of foodgrains.  The average farm family of
five mnembers works a holding of less than one ha of annual crops. Fruit
growing in addition to cultivation of annual crops nas becomne wore and more
popular over tihe past 20 years, and particularly in tile 1960's. In 1950
less tihan 1,00C) ha were under perennial fruits, while apples alone accounted
in 1969/70 for 23,000 hiia and the total area under perennial fruits has ex-
ceeded 39,0U0 na.  Whiile land resources for further expansion of fruit cul-
tivation are huge, long-term prospects for horticulture will largely depend
on the trends of producer prices (Annex 4).
Land Tenure
2.        iiP was created in 1950 by merging some 30 princely states.  It was
expanded by four districts takeni from Punjab in 1966 and it became a full
state onl.y in 1971.  During tne few years before thie merger of the princely
states, tile riglhts of the ryots (cultivators) were fairly clear and secure,
governeci by status and custom.  Land was generally heritable, saleable and
otherwise transferable property of tile ryots.  hiowever, most tenants were
at tile mercy of thie landlords.  Land tenure legislation between 1950 and 1954
irmproved tne righ1ts of tihe tenants.  Particularly Act No. 15, of 1954, fixed
a ceiling of one-tourtih of the produce for rent and provided for the acqui-
siLion of proprietary righits by tenants.  Finally, the hiindu Succession Act
of 19J6 brou 'nt about uniformity in dealing witn thie proprietary rifgtits of
iniheri ted lanel.  Univ cultivators whose rihlts on tihe land are secure nave
estabtishieei orcnards.  rmis group, commonlv termed ';land owners", consists
of pre-independence type land owners like Zamindari, Pattidari and dhaicriara,
as well as occupancy tenants wiho hlave used the land for at ieast 12 years.
Land Ise
3.         Ilic State ray be divided into four main agronormic zones, namely,
th-e low hill zone, the mid-htill zone, the high nill zone anci tiie dJry zone.
ITiese zonies are also Lypical fruiL production zones (see Appendix 1).  Elie
low iiill zone rainges up to 1,000 m and embraces most of the districts of



ANNEX 1
Page 2
Kangra and Bilaspur, as well as parts of Chamba, Simla and Sirmur. Tiie
mid-hill zone is a relatively narrow strip extending from altitudes of
1,000 to 1,500 m with soils varying in texture from loam to silt loam. Thiis
then progresses into the high hill zone at 1,500 to 2,700 m covering parts
of Chamba, Kangra, Mandi and Sirmur as well as the whole of Kulu and Mahasu.
The soils of this zone vary from silt loam to clay loam and the zone is most
suitable for temperate fruit production, apples being the main crop.  Finally,
along the north-eastern border at elevations of 2,400 to 3,000 m is the dry
hill zone, made up of the districts of Kinnaur and Lahaul/Spiti, together
witn the Pangi Tehsil of Cniamba District.  The area is barren and suitable
withiout irrigation only for the production of nuts and fruits such as almonds,
walnuts, grapes, prunes, and apricots; agriculture is little developed and
the area is the most backward in HP, tne districts of kinnaur and Lahaul/
Spiti not having a single town between them.
4.        All the nortlhern slopes are more moisture retentive than the
southern ones.  Consequently, the former are densely forested at the higiher
elevation and receive heavy snowfalls during the winter months, while the
southern exposures nave various amounts of deciduous growth and grassy cover.
iNearly 40% of the total area of the state is under forest (Table 1).  A
further 20% is permanent pasture and other grazing lands and less than 10%
is niet sown area, as compared witlh 45% for the wnole of India.
5.        Despite the overall importance of agriculture and ihorticulture,
forests are still the backbone of 1P's economy: they employ about 10% of
the labor force and generate 15% to 20% of the State's total cashk income.
The forest resource is of particular significance to the project in relation
to the supply of timber for packing cases (Annex 3).  rlaize, wheat and rice
are the major foodgrains cultivated (Table 2) and seed potatoes and fruits
the leading cash crops, wnicn are exported in considerable volume to the rest
of lnclia.
6.        Mixed farming and mixed cropping is the most coitimon cultural system
adopted by farmers.  In tile high hiills, orcharding is the main occupation
and has a dominating ana still increasing share in farmers' revenues; the
rearing of milchi cattle, poultry and sheep is limited. In the mid-hill zone
also, farmers are becoming more oriented towards cash crops and are planting
orchards along with the cultivation of foodgrains and ve-etables and the
rearing of milch cattle.  Even in the low hill and vallev areas, whiiclh are
most suited for the cultivation of food grains, farmers are adopting
orcharding, poultry andl dairy.
General Horticultural Development
7.        Althoughi many types of deciduous fruit trees grow well in UP and
certain species of pears and plums are indigenous, it is only within the
last few decades that commercial growing of deciduous fruits was started on
any scale.  The great interest in hP in apple growing rather than in other
deciduous fruits such as pear, peacAl, plum, apricot and cherry is demonstrated
by the fact that out of a total area of about 39,000 na devoted to all kinds



Ai.NEX 1
Page 3
of fruits - subtropical and nuts as well as deciduous - apples occupy about
23,000 ha (Tables 1 and 3). Probablv the major reasons for this preponderance
are that (i) climatically, at least above 1,500 m, the apple grows well, anid
that apart from competition from KashrLir, Uttar Pradesti and hilly areas in
southern India, there are few places where good quality apples can be commer-
cially grown in India; and (ii) the apple transports and stores better than
most other deciduous fruits, althougih it too must be handled carefully to
reachi market in good condition.  Among the makjor problems is the successful
transport of the fruit from the orchard - often in very isolated mountain
areas without any good roads.
8.         Probably, successful apple production in tiP is only possible due
to the influence of altitude on cliyate.  Aiearly the whole of the State is
very mountainous withl many deep ravines and valleys and it has been found
that, due to tine apple tree's need for adequate winter chilling, apples grow
best at altitudes of about 1,500 to 2,700 ri, wirere there is normally adequate
cold in thie winter to break bud dormancy. IHowever, temperatures are seldom
low enough to cause winter damiage to the trees. Spring frosts in certain
locations can be significanL in reducing the set of fruit.  Locationis witnl
restricted air drainage are most frequently subject to thlese crop losses.
Rainfall is generally adequate during the growing season.  Thlere are some
districts thiat could benefit from irrigation in the early summer mlonths uf
1iay and June.  heavy rains during hiarvest (August and September) can cause
harvest delay and fruit loss.  The principal weather hazard is hail whic:h
occurs fairly frequently.  Some apple-growing- areas receive ihail witLh greater
frequency than otners, but tile severitv of tihis problem has not been measured
for many newly planted areas.  dauage to fruits by hiail presents the greatest
cause of cullage.  Soils in tne apple producing areas appear well-suited to
apple production.  Trees of all ages have a good state of vegetative vigor.

10.       iIP Government.  hP Governnient has been advancing long-term horti-

with recovery starting in the sixth year and being completed in ten equal
annual installments. Loans are advanced against the security of land which
is hypothecated in the name of the Government. The loan limits are Rs 7,5U0
to individual growers and Rs 10,00()  to cooperatives.  In 1970-71 such loans
advanced by the State Government totalled &s 1.2 million.
11.       Cooperative Bankis.  The bulk of the credit needs of farmers are
met thirough the cooperative systera. un June 30, 1971, there were 3,756
cooperatives in the State with a membershiip of about 532,000.  On the same
date there were 2,547 agricultural credit societies, whose main functions
include the financing of seasonal crop operations, the supply of medium-term
credit and the provision of agricultural requisites and consumer goods at
reasonable rates.  During 1970-71, these cooperatives advanced loans to
a total value of Rs 49.3 million, while crop loans advanced by the coopera-
tive banks through cooperative societies amounted to Rs 15.8 million. Tihe
Land M4ortgage Bank makes orchard developmnent loans oni terms similar to those
granted by ilP Governmenit, but with a limit of Rs 3,750 per lha and an
interest rate of 7-1/2_o..
12.       Commercial banks.  At the end of 1971 thkere were 102 commercial

lhese banks currently are of little importance in the distribution of credit
to small farmers.  however, under the "Lead dank Scheme", formulated in 1969
by tne Keserve Bank of India as a concrete step towards the 'area approach"
to aevelopment, eachi of tnle Districts has been allocated to an individual
bank whose first taslk is to acquire the necessary Dasic data otn which to
evolve planis and programls for the extenision of adequate institutional cred it
on reasonable terms to the neglected and weaker sectors of the area.  In the

the role of a "consortium leader!'.
13.       .iew Credit Legislation.  The rnimaclial i-radesh Agricultural Credit
Operations and Mliscellaneous Provisions (3anks) biil was recently enacted.
Tnis law aims at a more adequate flow of credit for agricultural production
and development in the broadest sense of thie word througa commercial banks
and other inistitutional credit agencies. Thie law Ilot only provides for more
funds for increasing production of traditional agricultural activities, but
also for developing norticulture, marketirng, storage anu transport of
agricultural/aorticultural products, and the acquisition of agricultural/
iiorticultural implements and mac6inery.  It also entitles the liP Government
to greatly expand the proportion of farmers with rights of alienation of
land, incitiding tcue right to create a mortg,age on such land.  this newly
privileged group of farmers, will include, among others, non-occupancy tenants
and those lessees of tIP Government who are farming the leased land.  Commer-

Department of aorticulture
14.       General.  The main Government agency concerned with the administra-
tion of the apple industry is the State Department of hiorticulture (SDH)
which was establisihed under the Agricultural Production Commissioner in
September 1970 (Chart WB-7319). The Department has its headquarters in
Sirala and maintains offices in nine of the ten districts of HP. As of
June 1972, SDk included 762 posts of which 52 are at headquarters and the
remairnder in the field. SDtn personnel have long been involved with horti-
cultural production development throughout dP. This effort continues under
the two deputy directors of horticultural development and horticultural ex-
tension. Wit.h its established network of over 70 progeny-cum-demonstration
orchards and stations throughiout tuie State, SDii continues to demonstrate tne
performance of various kinds and varieties of fruits in different soil and
climatic zones, supplies pedigree and disease-free trees to farmers and
provides extension information on tree planting and new orchard development.
15.       Extension work could be expanded to cover production efficiency on
bearing orchards. Current production of approximately four tons per acre
of bearing orchard is low compareu to other apple oroducing areas. Applied
technology in the areas of fruit set, correction of alternate bearing,
prevention of fruit drop and disease control could increase production on
current bearing areas by at least 25%.  Most farmers with apple orchards
nave young, developing orchards.  They have little experience with apple
production technology on bearing trees and should have qualified extension
aid in applying this technology.
16.       Iinder SDUt's deputy directors in ciharge of horticultural extension,
senior officers at the field level are thie district horticultural officers.
In auditioni, horticultural developmient officers of the samle status are in
cnarge of the horticultural stations at Sajaura and Bokta.  Iiorticultural
inspectors perform tile function of block (suh-division of district) exten-
sion officers.  SDii is currently seeLin-, through.. the tiP Government, the
approval of additioaal inspector positions to give full statewide coverage
at tlne block level.
17.       For additional horticultural extension work tlere is need for
greater .iortLcultural technical specialization tnan is usually found at tihe
block level.  It is recoriviended that qualified horticultural extension
officers be added at the district level. Two to four specialists in each
district would help growers increase Lheir production ufficieiicy. The
districts of Mlahasu anid ilulu, withl greater numboers of apple growers, would
need four extension lhorticulturists and othur districts two to thiree.

(a)  lieasurement of weatner factors in different areas and

(b)  Tests for hiarvest rnaturi Ly anu post iiarvest tiandlin, -

and control of storage disorders.

(d)  Strengthiening of researcLi oni fruit setting factors - fruit
