Introducticn
1.        In HP the average apple orchard area is less than one ha z.nd, in
many cases, this is made Up of a number of plots. Standards of management
of the orchards are largely satisfactorz.  Farmers are usually acquainted
with the techniques of fertilizer appli,:ation, crop protection, etc. and
these practices have been standardized. Most farm operations are urdertaken
manually, utechanization not being feasible to any extent due to tne topo-
grapny. Family labor is supplemented by hired workers, particularl) by
seasonal workers between July and Octobear. rhe main sources of hirEd labor,
apart from local, are seasonally immigrating N'epalese and libetan refugees.
CultivatiorL Techniques
2.        Planting.  Due to the steep taiuntain slopes and heavy rairnfall
in hP, terracing is usually necessary t) avoid soil erosion as well as to
keep the soil covered with vegetation. If the land is verv steep, terracing
becomes very expensive and in suclh case; trees are mainly planted oa. the
contours.  In general, orchard lands ara found in grass cover with (nly a
small circle of cultivated soil around ::ile trunk of each tree. oepEnding
on the characteristics of the particula- location including, inter flia,
gradient, soil, rainfall and exoosure t, the sun, most newly-plantec trees
(especially in drv and warm locations) require watering duringt the first
two years, and benefit from tihe applicazion of mulches covering the cultivated
areas surrounding them.  Mlostly the gra 3s of the orchard is cut by Iand and
used as a mulch, but in a few cases, wh2re possible, mechanical mowi fng is
practiced.  Sometimes pine needlles or dey oak leaves are brought frcm the
forest as a mulca.  This mulch cover both preserves soil moisture .-nId
improves the nutrient status of the soil.  Apart from mulching ann irrigation
of newly planted trees, there is a need for further irrigation of oichards
in many locations, especially at the lower elevations or in tLe dry areas.
3.        Planting is carried out as we3ticr permits in autumn to ectrlv
spring, and the usLial planting tecliniques are used.  Thiere is a wide differ-
ence of spacing, ranging from 250 to 75) trees per tha, the smaller itoldings
tendiug towards the closer plantings.  tiowever, most orchlards accommouate
250 to 400 trees per ha.  Intercropping with cashl crops such as peas, beans,
potatoes or wheat is frequently carried out on the more level areas when
the trees are young, as the apple crop ,ill not vield returns to tnh
farr,mers for five to six years.



AiNEX 2
Page 2
4.        Pruning, necessary for development, growtih and yield, is done at
two stages: (i) wnen the tree is young to develop a proper structure and
shape (the form of tree desired is usually that w-ith a central leader or
modified central leader), and (ii) when the tree reaches its bearing state,
where the objective is to maintain a balance between growth and cropping.
In general, most trees are pruned adequately, but in sor,me cases over-.y
hLeavy pruning has delayed cropping. Young trees of tile Delicious va-^ieties,
which are thie main ones grown, are generally very upright in growth. This
makes for delayed bearing urntil the weiFht of the crop spreads the branches
to a more horizontal level.  Special methods of pruning and managemeat couid
be used to assist this process to induce earlier bearing, including tihe
bending-down and tying of branches; and their ringing and girdling, while
pruning sliould be kept to a miniumn whet. thie basic framework of the young
tree has been formed.  It is possible t. use thiese techniques on trees,
seedlings and vigorous rootstocks, and obtain considerably earlier cropping.
5.        ilanure and Fertilizers.  Usua'ly farmyard manure, phospihate and
potassic fertilizer and half the amnount of nitrogenous fertilizers annually
applied are applied in late autumn before the snow, and the remaining portion
of nitrogen in Mlarch.  At 250 trees per ha, SDh  nas recommended the follow-
ing annual amounts of manure and fertil,zers per ha:  about 25 tons of or-
ganic nanure, 900 kg of calciurr. ammoniumn nitrate, 550 kg of stuperphcsphate
and 280 kg of muriate of potassium. In practice, it is often difficult for
the farmer to carry out this system, as frequently only compound fertilizers
are available (e.g., .itrophoska containing N.P.K. in a 15:15:15 ratio).  It
is also often difficult to obtain adequate supplies of potassic fertilizer.
6.        Pollination.  MAost apple varieties need cross pollination for the
setting of heavy crops, and in many orc-ards, due to prevailing low tempe!ra-
ture at time of blossom, exposure to winds, lack of adequate sources; cf pollen
and pollinating insects, much reduced yields are obtained.  In riP, 1:he usual
practice is to plant one pollinator in nine plants (third plant in every
third row), or every alternate plant in the third row is tihe nollinitor (1.5.
pollinators).  In areas of inclement weather up to 20-25Z pollinatiiF trees
are reconmended.  Closely related varieties, such as Delicious and its various
sports, are unsatisfactory as pollinatcrs for one anothier, but GOldrn. eliciou'
is a good pollinator for the whiole Delicious Group, and Jonathan is also very
effective.  to assist pollination, bee hives silould be introducedi in1to tlhe
orcnards, but this is rarely done. only well-populated ihives are effective,
and there should be at least five of tl-ese per hectare.
7.        Application of Pesticides.  The problen of spraying machlinles
suitable for work on the steep mountainsides where most of the orchards
are planted needs some attention.  Miost of the orchards are now sprayed
using small foot sprayers (pedal pump) which are fairly easv to maove up and
down tile terraces and slopes, but can onily spray one-third of a ha of orciard
a day.  Considerable problems exist irn spraying thie larger orenards.  In tne
past, engine-powered spraying machines were imported from the US, but witn
ti-mne and shortage of spare parts more and more of tiiese have become
immobilized.  These machiines weigh about 30 kg, are movable by two nmen an"

may be carried up and down the terraces.  By using a 15-25 m spray h)se,
they can spray three terraces without mcving the machine up or down.
Further supplies of such sprayers, prefErably made in India, would greatly
help improve the quality of the fruit. It should also be noted thlat the
winter oil spray - the major essential Epray - must be given at hiigh
volume, and motorized knapsack sprayers. being only for low volume spraying,
are unsuitable.
Varieties and Yields
8.        Varieties.  A wiide range of apple varieties have been tested in
hiP and many grown on a commercial scale. but over the years the older
varieties, mainly introduced from the United Kingdom, have been superseded
by American varieties, primarily Delicious and its various sports ard Golden
Delicious. At present, about 90% of the production is of Delicious strains,
namely Royal (Starking) Delicious (45%m)  Red Delicious (20%), Rich-a-red (5%)
and Golden Delicious (20%). The remain:.ng 10% consists of Red June, iWorces-
ter Permain, Kashmeri Red, Jonathan, Kinig of Pippins, Granny Smith, Red Gold,
ilaharaji (Versifield), Ambri, -Len Davis, Williams Favorite, Red AstTachan,
Winter Commercial, Penoni and others.  In recent years nearly all the plant-
ings have been of Royal and Red Delicious with Colden Delicious as the riain
pollinator - this yellow apple is more and more difficult to rarlket and
varieties like Tvdexman's Early Worceste: or Late Orange could well le used
as pollinators.  Appendix 1 summarizes tihe characteristics relevant to
fresh marketing for the main apple varieties of HP.
9.        The external color of the fruit is crucial in determining both its
grade and market value.  Tne elevation at whichl tile fruit is grown is an
important factor in determining its color, and the best color of the J)clicious
sports is considered to be a deep bright red.  Royal, if grown over 2,000 m,
may become a dull black color, wnile Red Delicious is much greener at low
elevations.  Rich-a-red is also not advisable at hiilher elevations.  Preferably,
therefore, Royal should be grown at 1,300 to 2,000 m and Red Delicious at
2,000 to 2,700 m.  Golden Delicious does well at all elevations,  These
general rules are subject to modification in districts such as iZulu where
local climate factors have an influence and in Kinnaur where even Golden
Delicious exhibits a red tint.
10.       Yields.  Many factors determine the annual yields of an orchard,
the more important ones being spacing, variety, elevation, weather riazards,
pests and diseases, and cultivation arid orcioard management. The troes
normally start to produce in the sixtih year. A typical satisfactorily
managed one ha orchar.l develops the following yield pattern (adverse
weather, pests and disease effects being averaged out):

In some areas hail can cause the total Loss of crops once in three )ears
and in others only once in ten years. The effect of spring frosts is also
very variable in the different areas anl elevations. Yields of up to 18
tons per hla have been recorded, even fo- large orcihards.  However, Even
these top yields are far below the averige yields of major apple-producing
countries like Japan (24 tons/lha) and France (21 tons/ha), where apple
growing has, been a well established indistry for a long time.  A reasonable
target for production policy would be t3 increase average yields by 25"'
(to 15-16 tons/ha on full bearing orchards) over a four to five yeai. period
through improved cultivation practices.
Harvesting

through early October.  This loni' harve3t season is caused by two major factors:
(1) great dlifferences in orchard elevation and exposure; and (2) va-:ieties
and strains which miature at different times.  Clart WB-7438 illustrates the
general harvest Deriod for HP and related volures available durinc! ,he
harvest period.  M1inor varieties (Red June, King of Pippins and Worcester
Perrmain) are harvested in early to mid-July.  Re6 Delicious and Golden Deli-
cious are ilarvested frotm July to late September.  The majority are iiarvcsted
in th-e montlh of August withi peak of lharvest beginning in mid-August.  Late
varieties whic'L accounted for less thiran five percent of crop, (sucll as Granny
Smith) are harvested in late Septemtder anl early October.
12.        In any one apple producing area, suchl as tlhe Kotgarh, Kuli or
.Nagar areas, tiie harvest period for the mrvajor varieties of Re` ancd  ,olc-len
Delicious apples is three to four weeks .  Thae peak of harvest would usually
come in the second week of tiais area liurvest period.  Tihe peak day :f LarvesL
could reacii 20, of tne total volume foi tLiis group of apples, but in mnost

Apppl_e Production
14.       Apple production in HP can on]y be estimated as there has  een no
accounting of apples produced.  Records obtained from SDII consist of numbers
of trees delivered to apple producers fcr planting each year. These records
include trees sur-lied from Governmenit-sponsored as well as private nurseries.
Nlumbers of trec.s delivered to sub-geographic areas (patwars) are also avail-
able. Numbers of trees supplied and est:imates of new areas planted, by
patwars, are available only in major apple producing areas.  For further
analysis of production by sub-geographic areas, surveys of apple plantings
in the remaining apple growing patwars must be made. Centers of current and
future apple production can be identified only in the areas where apple
plantings by patwars are available. With these records, tihe mission Tnade
some tentative estimates of apple area and production (Table 1).  SDh com-
mented that these estimates, particularly the production1 projections, were
fairly conservative.  In fact, these projections do not at all take into
account likely future production improvements like better pruning, increasing
use of fertilizers and more timely iharvesting dates.
15.       In spite of their conservative cliaracter, thie estimates in Iable 1,
impressively demonstrate the tremendous past and expected growth of Llte apple
in1dustry in hiP in the 1960s and 1970s. It is, in fact, only in tihe last
decade thiat the industry has attracted large numbers of small-scale farners.
As roads were opened up, thie opportunities for apple cultivation in areas
generally unsuitable for other types of enterprise were realize1.  'iTis, coupled
with tne existence of a vast protected market throu,ghout the cotuntry witn its
attractive prices, and withi competent and aggressive promotional activities
carried out by SDLi, 1.P has StiTnUlated a rapid increase in plantings.  Estimated
area under apples increased about ei,.ht times from 196nl to 1970, (fromn
3,000 h.a to 26,300 ha) or on average, 23/% a year, and total productioli more
than six times (from about 10,OCO tons to 76,.030 tons) or oni average, 21;, a
year.  Area increase exceeded production increase, as in the late 1960s new
plantings were particularly heavy anT  most of tie apple orchards (65 7) were
non-bearing in 1970.  When, in 1973, all tlhe existing orc6iards will be
bearing, total production will be about 250,00F) tons, double th1e present.
production.  In tile 197D-78 period prodlucLion increase will SJlow downv  to
12i. per annum.  Tne expansion ihas beuen ana will continue to be, largely
concentrated in tlie districts of 'iahasu, Kulu, Mandi and Sirmur, where the
environmental conditions are nost favorable. These four districts in
1970/71 accounted for 94T.' of total productionl anu llahasu alone for 4'U-.

Economics oE Apple Production
16.       Initiation of apple growing has essentially been the first step of
riany smaall-scale farmers into tne casn economy.  M4ost of thlem are practicing
mixed farri i,g (Annex 1, para 6) . Orchards have beeni developed mainly on
former pastures or unusedl land, so that the area under annual crops aas
hardly been reduced. Orchard developmert takes about five years and requi res
neavy capital and/or labdr investments (T.'ale 2 and 3), while production starts
only in the sixtih year at the modest rate of two tons of fresh apple3 per ha.
Only in the tenth year does an orchard ieach its full bearing state (10 tons/ha).
With the existing cost/price structure, the cashi flow of a typical one ha
orchard becomes positive only in the ninthl year.  Even after allowance for
family labor, revenues to a small-scale grower accrue in year six.
17.       Althou,,.. HP Government has extendedc many orchard development
credits of Rs ;,375 per ha (Annex 1, papa 10) for several years, growers
nave needed additional funds of Rs 4,00(1 to Rs 9,000 to establish a one Ila
orchard. Thie multiplication of liP's ari a under apples in the 1960s lhas shown
tiiat there is apparently no shortage of funds to meet these additioral cash
requirements.  In many cases growers bo-row money from non-institutional
sources and/or use savings they have ea2ned outside their farms (public works,
lumbering, etc.).  On the other hand, capital requirements for traditional
annual cropping are comparably negligibLe and most cash inputs flow back to
the farmer at the end of the season.
18.       Even recurrent production cos,s for a one ha apple orcharc. (Table 4)
exceed the average output per ha of annial crops (Annex 1, Table 2). Labor
costs alone account for about 70a of re:urrent production cost.  Anc: in
physical terms, labor requirements are in the order of 450 man-days per annum
and equal those for most intensive irri:ated vegetable growin- in the Punjab.
To meet tnese high labor requirements mnny small growers even emplolv both
(semi-) permanent and casual workers.  Quite a few of the latter imnigrrate
regularly from outside the State, particularly from Nepal.  Thfese UIopalese
are highly esteemed for tiheir skill and perseverance at carrying cases of
apples from the orchards to the roadheai.  In addition, mnany of them earn
additional income from c-rowing seed potatoes on seasonally rented land.
19.       Although apple production requires a hich rapital investm2nt and
recurrent expenditure, and a five-year investment period with returns starting
only in the sixtn year, the financial rate of return calculated at 24?, for
a one lha model orchard appears extremely attractive (Table 2). Virtually
all returns are cash and net revenues to a farmer's family from a fall
bearing one ha orchardl amount to Rs 7,(0't per annum, while the avera3e
annual gross output per ha of net sown area for most seasonal crops is well
below Rs 2,000 in iHP (Annex 1, Table 2).  The growth of the apple industry
has dramatically increased the net revenues of tnousands of farmers, although
it is basedi on land for whiich obviously no alternative use exists.  It has
become HP's most important growth industry and h-as helpedi the state's
economy keep up with the growth of the national econonmy.  In addition, the



N-IN   -2
Page 7
industry has considerably contributed to reducing unemployment and under-
employment and it will increasingly continue to do so commensurate w.tii
growing production. Improved production and harvesting techniques as
recommended in this Annex will further stimulate apple output and better
quality.  iiowever, to prevent growers' incomes from stagnating or even
declining because of the expected oversupply (Annex 11) processing and narlzeting
services are urgently required. Therefore, the proposed project concentrates
on providing such services.  A second phase project rlight focus on p:oduction
promotion, too.

INDIA
HINACHAL PRADESH APPLE PROCESSING AND MARKETING PROJECT
Present Apple Grading, Packing,
Storage and Processing Activities
Grades
1.        The apples are picked on the basis of their color characteristics
and are classified into three quality grades: A, B, and C. Grade A fruits
of each variety are fully colored anu free from blemishes, while Grade B
includes less colored fruits with some marks; all other fruits having less
color, more blerdsl.es and damage, togethier witih Pitto size fruit of Grade A
and small to Pittu of Grade B, are grouped as Grade C.
2.        In normal years, it is estimated that 60% of the total production
is Grade A, 20% Grade B, and 20% Grade C. Jlaturally, however, the average
yield per unit of land, and the size and grade distribution fluctuate widely
from year to year. For example, due to hail storms, the proportion of
Grade A in one of the districts was only 25% in 1971.
Grading
3.        Grading of apples is done mostly by hand in the orchard, in
simple sheds, by the same workers who pick the fruit. The bruised or
danaged fruits are first culled out from the whole lot, and the rernaining
fruits are graded into seveni size grades, namely, Super, Extra Large, Large,
Hedium, Small, Lxtra Small, and Plitto.  Tne minirmum diameters for the
various size-grades are given in the foliowing table:
Size Grades of thimachal 'Pradesh Apples
Name of Grade              Size of Fruit        Lstimated Distribution /1
(miniMum diameter in mm)         (percentage)
Super                           81                          .12
Lxtra Large                     75                         3.02
Large                           69                        23.78
.Tedium                         63                        31.85
Small                           57                        29.69
Lxtra Srimall                   51                         9.00
Pitto                           45 and less                2.54
/1   Simple averages for Royal and Red Delicious derived from limited
samples.



ANNi£X 3
Page 2
4.        The size is generally measured by holding the fruit in the left
hand and then placing the right hand fingers in the space left between the
thumb and the middle finger. Very simple mechanical graders are now used
by several leading growers. The size grades are well established and fairly
uniform except in the case of new entrants who are unfamiliar with the
practice. There are, however, irregularities in grading and packing, par-
ticularly where the fruits are packed by the speculative traders known as
"pre-harvest contractors". Fruits of different varieties are packed in the
same box, and some of the unscrupulous ones practice "topping" in which
inferior fruits are laid on the bottom layers and are covered up with
superior quality fruits on the top layer. Hence the practice of cumbersome
and time-consuming steps in the wholesale procedure in which every prospec-
tive buyer inspects and scrutinizes the contents of many boxes from each
lot of apples being offered for sale.
Packing
5.        Packing follows grading, frequently on the same day, and is per-
formed in the same locality. Fruit is packed in "standard wooden boxes" of
16 to 18 kilograms capacity, or in "half boxes" of 8 to 9 kilograms. The
actual dimensions of the boxes, however, vary to accommodate fruits of
different size grades. The specifications of apple boxes are given below:
Specification of Standard Apple Packing Cases
Number of       Number of
in cm           Apple Grades           Layers           Fruit
1.  45.7 x 30.5 x 25.4   Extra Large                 3            60 -  66
Small                     4                 140
Pitto                     5           150 - 300
2.  45.7 x 30.5 x 27.9   Super                       3            50 -  57
ledium                    4           112 - 120
Extra Small               5                 200
3.  45.7 x 30.5 x 30.5   Large                       4                   96
Most commonly the fruits are wrapped in old newspapers, or "raddi", with
sheets of paper and either pine needles or wood wool between the layers.
6.        Apple packing cases are still nade exclusively of wood, mostly
kail and rai, and the material for them in the form of "shooks" is supplied
directly to farmers by a large numtber of small sawmills located along tne
main roads in the apple producing areas. The sole purpose of these sawmills
is to meet the requirements of the apple industry and when the apple season
is over tney cease operations. They hiave been supported by the attractive
industrial loans granted by the Industries Department, and by the provision
of 25 to 30 trees eachi year at concessionary price of ls 0.70 per cu ft
allocated by the Forestry Department of HP. In view of the concessions
so made to the sawmills, the government exercises some control over the



ANNEX 3
Page 3
prices of packing cases, which, however, are influenced to some extent by
the demand and supply situation.  During the 1971-72 season, premiums were
paid over the fixed level of Rs 3.00 per case due to the bumper harvest.
The margin on each case must be quite high to make the small-scale operations
attractive.
7.        The Government is currently concerned at the depletion of the
forest resources and SDH has been testing cartons which might substitute
for the traditional wooden cases. Considerable progress has been made in
this field and the principal remaining obstacles are that the carton remains
slightly more expensive than the wooden case and thle quality needs some
improvement. While the introduction of cartons will inevitably require
important changes in the manner of transporting and auctioning apples, they
appear quite feasible, provided that a sufficiently dynamic marketing
organization, such as is envisaged in the project, is established. Also
the possibility of using boxes made of thin wood slices bound with wire is
being explored and looks promising.
8.        ine other essential materials for apple packing are wood wool,
raddi (newspapers), wrapping paper, nails, wires and strapping equipment,
whicih are supplied by local traders, forwarding agents, pre-harvest con-
tractors, and commission agents. The total cost of packing materials per
case of 18 kg is estimated to be Rs 5.17, consisting of Rs 3.00 for wooden
box, Rs 0.30 for 100 gms of nails, Rs 0.30 for 300 gms of wood wool, Rs 0.85
for 500 grLs of wrapping paper, Rs 0.65 for wires, and Rs 0.07 for stencilling
ink.
Storage Mlethod
9.        In the crop year 1970/71, only about 12,000 tons, or about 6.0%
of the total marketed apples were put in cold storage facilities, while the
remainder were sold to consumers shortly after harvest. IWrhile the off-
season market appears quite attractive, not enough apples are now stored
to extend tihe mar'rketing period partly because the cold storage facilities
currently available are intended primarily for potatoes. These storages are
not ideally suited to handle apples, which require lower temperature levels
and more exacting humidity controls than they can provide.  By building cold
storage facilities designed to meet apple storage requirements the apple
marketing period can be considerably extended.
Cold Storage Installations
10.       The combined capacity of refrigerated cold storages in India is
estimated to be 1.5 million m tons. The degree of utilization is exception-
ally high, and the facilities are used mainly for storing potatoes (92%) , but
also seafood, meat and meat products, dairy products and textiles. The
majority of cold storages are between 1,000-5,000 tons capacity, and are
owned by tdie private sector which apparently earns very high returns on this
investment. Lxcept for tihe control ecuipment, almost all other components
such as compressors, motors, insulation material, and refrigerators are



ANNEX 3
Page 4
manufactured locally.  About 87/% of the cold storages use ammonia as refri-
gerant, and the remaining 13% use Freon 12 or 22.  Of the combined capacity
of about 1.5 million tons, 566 installations with a total capacity of
841,000 tons are situated in northwest India where apples are produced and,
to a great extent, consumed:
Chandigarh           (  2 units)             7,000 tons
Delhi                ( 35 units)            36,700 tons
Haryana              ( 49 units)            51,900 tons
Punjab               ( 97 units)            92,440 tons
Jammu and Kashmir   (  2 units)              1,100 tons
Uttar Pradesh        (376 units)           652,000 tons
Total           (566 units)          841,000 tons
About 75% of this capacity is used for potatoes.
Fruit Processing
11.       In 1969, India had 12 plants producing fruit juices and pulps,
nine plants producing juice concentrates, beverages and synthetic syrups,
and four concerns producing jams, jellies and marmalades. The Indian fruit
processing industry is in its infancy, mainly because the great majority
of processing plants are situated far from fruit production areas. The
chief fruits processed are the mango, pineapple and citrus, and detailed
information on the output is given in Table 1. W;hile in 1969/70 the total
volume of processed fruit was over 33,000 tons, valued at about Rs 93
million, the apple products were only 1,500 tons (less thani 1% of the total
supply of marketed apples) with an estimated value of about Rs 5 million.
12.       SDh has been carrying out pilot fruit processing activities in
recent years, and their products include apple juice, jams made of the
various locally produced fruits including apple, and canned peaches, etc.
13.       Tne market potential for fruit drinks appears very bright.  Sale
of soft drinks in India has been growing at phenomenal rates in recent years.
About 104,000 tons of Coca Cola valued at Rs 170 million was marketed in
1970. Other major ready-to-serve beverages were 1,902 tons of mango drinks
valued at Rs 3.6 million, 360 tons of pineapple drinks valued at Rs 660,000
and about 300 tons of orange and other ready-to-serve beverages valued at
Rs 4.7 million for the same year.

INDIA
HIMACHAL PRADESH APPLE PROCESSING AND MARKETING PROJECT
Present Marketing System of Himachal Pradesh Apples
A. INTRODUCTION
Production and Market Prospects
1.        During the five-year period from 1966 to 1971, Indian apple
production has increased at an average rate of approximately 307% a year
and, considering the plantings coming to maturity, is expected to grow at
approximately 17% for at least the next five years. Although the total
consumption of apples in India, about 270,000 tons in 1970/71, is only
about 1/2 kg per capita, compared with 10 to 30 kg in developed countries,
and sufficient market potential exists to absorb the increasing production,
unless appropriate measures are taken to improve the lagging marketing
facilities, the seasonal gluts in the market are expected to intensify and
the prices to suffer.
Producing States and Varieties
2.        There are only three states in India where climatic conditions
are suitable for apple culture, namely HP, Jammu and Kashmir, and Uttar
Pradesh. As shown in Table 1, of the total 1970 production, Jammu and
Kashmir accounted for 157,000 tons, HP 76,000 tons, and Uttar Pradesh
40,000 tons. The degree of competition between the apples from different
states is limited as their marketing seasons are different and only overlap
for a short period. The Uttar Pradesh apples are earliest to arrive in the
market and peak in July-August, followed by the HP apples peaking in
September-October, and by the Jammu and Kashmir apples forming the last
peak of the season in October-November.
Competition witn Other Fruits
3.        There is only limited competition between apples and other fruits
in India. While India produces significant quantities of mangoes
(7,000,000 tons), bananas (3,200,000 tons), guava (90,000 tons), mandarins
(500,000 tons), grapes (170,000 tons), litchi (90,000 tons), pineapples
(75,000 tons), and other less important fruits, apples remain the most
popular of all. 'ine only fruits available in quantities during the apple
season are bananas and mandarins which do not substitute for apples in the
market.
Prices
4.        The general wholesale apple price level has remained relatively
hiigh so far at about Rs 2.00 per kg on the average, albeit fluctuating widely



ANNEX 4
Page 2
from the lowest of Rs 0.81 in November 1971, to the highest of Rs 5.55 in
Harch 1971. The price pattern follows the normal trend of relatively high
levels at the beginning of the season followed by declines as heavy supplies
reach the market. This is followed by some mild price recovery until the
end of the harvest. During the off-season, prices for cold storage apples
climb steadily, and, towards the end of the storage life of the apple,
relatively small consignments bring unusually high prices. The full po-
tential of the off-season market in the main consuming centers has not yet
been exploited. On the whole, prices have shown a tendency to decline
over recent years as increased supplies came onto the market.
International Trade and Comparative Advantage
5.        India has neither imported nor exported apples on a commercial
scale in recent years. The cost of production, conservatively estimated
at about Rs 750 per ton of marketable apples at the farmgate before
packing, compares favorably with prices prevailing in the world market.
Thie prospects for export are, however, not good. The domestic market is
too attractive, and the infrastructures required for apple export at inter-
nationally competitive costs such as packing materials industries, refrigerated
trucking services, waterfront cold storages suitable for holding apples and
a competent trade organization to carry out international marketing are not
in sight.
Processitg of Apples
6.        Only a very small fraction of total apple production is processed.
In 1970, the amount marketed in India was 17 tons of jam, 200 tons of juice,
and 873 tons of preserves valued at Rs 75,000, Rs 1,721,000 and Rs 3,114,000
respectively. The total input of apples for processing these products is
estimated about 2,400 tons; this is only one percent of total Indian apple
production for the corresponding year. There have been many unsuccessful
attempts at conmercial apple processing but in every case, the undertaking
was too small to warrant the large financial outlays required for successful
merchandise promotion.
Lagging Marketing Facilities
7.        The development of marketing facilities has not kept pace with
the rapid expansion of production. 14ost of the roads that have opened up
the producing areas are still of inferior standard, resulting in frequent
road blockages caused Dy rain and landslides during the monsoon when apples
are harvested and transported to market. All of the fruit is still graded
and packed by individual farmers without any quality control. The packed
fruit is left by the roadsides for collection, usually without adequate
protection from weather. Its onward movement to the consignee commission
agent isiundertaken by numerous local forwarding agents withi little attempt
at coordination other than that arranged, to a limited extent, by SDH.
Iost of the fruit then travels by truck to urban centers as far as Bombay,
Calcutta, Delhi, Madras, ijyderabad and Bangalore with one or more trans-
shipments, exposure to weather and lack of coordination of movement. The
/



ANNEX 4
Page 3
complexities of the stages through which the apples pass in the present
system are portrayed in World Bank Chart 7208.
Scope for Improving Marketing
8.        There is considerable scope for expanding the market for fresh
and cold storage apples, and for improving the quality and reducing the
cost of marketing services. Streamlining the present apple marketing
system with its cumbersome, costly and time-consuming steps is clearly
desirable and feasible. It will significantly reduce the time and cost
of marketing and prevent the quality deterioration of the fruit in transit.
There is also scope for commercial utilization of the large quantities of
cull apples, at present wasted, as raw material for juice concentrates,
which would provide substantial sources of additional income to the apple
growers.
Malpractices in Present System
9.        1r4ost of the apples marketed in urban markets in India are
consigned to commission agents who do the selling at the wholesale level
on behalf of the shippers, who are in most instances the apple growers,
and, to a much lesser extent, the traders commonly called  "pre-harvest
contractors." Theoretically, the role of the commission agent is to pro-
vide the selling facilities, physical and institutional, and to act as
the consignee for a fixed commission of about 7% of the whiolesale value,
whicii the growers consider quite exorbitant. Practically, however, many
of tne commission agents play the questionable dual role of commission agents
and speculative buyers, leaving scope for widespread malpractices at the
expense of the consignors.  After deducting advances made, the commission
agent remits the proceeds to the grower, often after long delays.
Alternative Ilarketing Channel
10.       liost of the growers are apprehensive and even hostile towards
tile commission agents, but they hiave no alternative under the existing
system.  Of the several categories of allegations made by the growers
against the commission agents on underhanded practices, some are ground-
less and trivial, but some are warranted and call for rectification. A
general reformd th-1rough enforcement of rules and the regulation of agricultural
produce marketing does not, however, appear to be a practical solution.
Entry of a conmercially oriented marketing organization competitive with
the existing commission agents can be expected to exert sufficient checks
on the alleeed abuses. At least it will offer an alternative to the growers,
and serve as an instrument of quality control, packing and transport cost
reduction, and of coordinated marketing with which the comnaission merchants
have not been concerned.  The legitimate, proper, and, in some respects,
constructive functions performed by the commission agents should not be
overlooked. Wlat is at present lacking is the instrument for preventing
malpractices and for carrying out the rationalization of marketing operations
as an integrated scheme. Conventional and project marketing channels are
illustrated in Chart WB-7208 (R).



ANlNEX 4
Page 4
Utilization of Cull Apples for Processing
11.       The cullage of apples in India, and HP in particular, where hail
damage can be devastating, is usually as high as 20%. The total volume of
culls in HP alone is estimated to be about 20,000 tons in 1971 but
little use is made of them. By processing culls into juice concentrates, not
only can additional food supplies be made available but the growers' incomes
can also be significantly increased. According to the analysis given in
Annex 11, it would be feasible to operate an apple juice concentrate plant
on a commercially viable basis.
B. TRANSPORTATION
Roads
12.       The most serious hurdle in marketing HP apples has been the
absence of adequate road networks from orchards to roadhead and from there
to the collection points. The limited facilities that now exist are vul-
nerable to weather and recurrent disruptions. The movement of fruit from
the mountainous areas of hP to Delhi and onward involves complicated
transport operations. In the first place, the fruits have to be moved
from farms, usually on precipitous sites, to collection points on the
roadhead where mechanized transport of some sort is possible. Moreover,
since most of the roads are narrow and tortuous, transshipment to larger
vehicles once the produce reaches the plairs is economically desirable.
13.       The terminal markets are far from the producing areas in HP.
From Simla, the distance to Delhi is 350 km, to Calcutta 1,800 km, Borbay
1,800 kmn, Bangalore, 2,300 km, Ilyderabad 1,700 km, and Hadras 2,430 km.
During the fresh apple marketing months, the temperature is usually very high
along the roads leading to these terminal markets. It ranges from 25 to 34°C
for Delhi, 25 to 32°C for Bombay and Calcutta, 20 to 30°C for Hyderabad,
24 to 340C for lHadras, and 19 to 28°C for Bangalore. From Delhi, it takes
about four to six days for trucks to reach Calcutta or Bombay.
14.       Akpart from the physical conditions and the distances referred to,
the transport situation is immensely complicated by the administrative
regulations of intra-state and inter-state road transport. In most
areas of HP, road transport of both passengers and goods are fully nationalized,
but vehicles of private truck operators are "attached" to augment the
government fleet.  hiowever, in a few areas of itP, licensed public carriers
in the merged areas may ply freely in those areas, but are required to pay
a high fee (Rs 6,000 a year) applicable in the old areas if they wisih to
extend their field of operations. Under a new reciprocal agreement for tine
northern zone involving flihar, Haryana, Jamnu and Kashmir, Punjab, IJajasthan,
Uttar Pradesh, West Bengal and the Union Territory of Chandigarh, however,
the whole syster. is expected to be considerably liberalized, hopefully,
sometime in 1973. In particular, each state may admit up to 200 vehicles
at any tirae of another state on a reciprocal basis.



Ni'4EX 4
Page 5
15.       Impediments to inter-state movement of trucks have resulted in
the transshipment of apples at different points, which inevitably involves
delay and costly damage and wastage to fruits. Trucks from EIP only go to
Simla or Parwanu near Kalka, from where the forwarding agents arrange for
further trucking to Delhi or, in some instances, to other distant terminal
markets. If the fruits are consigned to other terminal markets, the forward-
ing agents at Parwanu or Simla consign the goods only to Delhi where another
set of forwarding agents takes over and make onward shipping arrangements.
16.       As shown in Table 2, the cost of transportation per case of apples,
18 kg net, from the producing areas has been estimated to be about Rs 5.35
to Delhi, Rs 9.90 to Calcutta, and Rs 10.55 to Bombay including the forward-
ing charges, octroi 1/, loading and unloading charges. The spoilage and
losses are not accounted for in these costs, and they are perhaps as costly
as the cash outlay on transportation.
Railways
17.       Railway transport has not been popular in shipping apples.  HP
does not have any broad gauge line, and the nearest one is at Kalka, Haryana.
This has been the decisive factor in using trucks for apple transport.
Moreover, the railway freight rates are, due to the classification of apples
as "'expensive fruits", higher than the truck rates. IThile the unit cost
may be reduced by using rail cars for bulk shipping, no large-scale marketing
attempt has been made. There are three additional disadvantages in using
railways for apple transportation:
(a) it involves transshipments at the sidings at the origin
as well as the destination;
(b)  it requires the consignor to pay the freight charge in
advance, while the existing trucking system makes it
possible to have the consignee make the advance; and
(c)  it damages more fruits tnan truck transport due to
rougher handling.
C.  IUSTITUTIONAL FRALfWORK OF IARKETINIG
Legal Aspects of Apple Marketing
18.       Except for the unsuccessful attempts being made at the Hyderabad
market to regulate trading practice of the fruit under the ilyderabad
Agricultural Liarketing Act of 1963, apple markets in India are unregulated.
1/   Municipal tax.



AINEX 4
Page 6
The Agricultural Produce (Grading and Marking) Act, 1937, (with rules made
prior to 31st December, 1959,) provides procedures and standards for grading
and marking agricultural produce covering 38 items, including apples. It
specifies the required qualities and grades of the produce but as far as the
apple is concerned the law has remained unenforced and no quality control has
been practiced. The Hyderabad Agricultural Marketing Act of 1963, attempts
to regulate the wholesale practice in the city, but, very little has been
accomplished and the Hyderabad market operates essentially the same as other
major markets.
MIultiplicity of Intermediaries
19.       Apple marketing in India is characterized by the dominance of
rniddlemen at different levels, involving the pre-harvest contractors,
forwarding agents, commission agents-wholesalers, l1ashakhoras (sub-whole-
salers), retailers. and, in case of the remote terminal markets, repetition
once or twice of thie wholesale process. The existence of so many inter-
m-nediaries causes apple prices to snowball as the fruit passes through each
transaction. Direct links between the growers and the terminal markets are
very limited, principally because: (i) marketing centers are very remote
from the producing areas; (ii) production is largely in the hands of small
growers whose in(lividual output is too shall to warrant their own marketing
away from the producing areas; (iii) the high prices of apples realized in
the past relative to the cost of production (not necessarily relative to
tiie consumer prices), have contented the growers and there has been little
pressure for an organized attempt at reducing the multiplicity of inter-
mediaries; and (iv) many of the growers have had to borrow money for apple
production and other cash needs from apple traders.
Pre-harvest Contractors
20.       The small growers, the inexperienced ones in particular, sell
their apples at the time of fruit setting to speculators called "pre-
harvest contractors," to whom orchiards are leased for one or two years. by
this practice the novice may learn from the contractor how to harvest,
grade, pack and consign the fruits. He may also hedge his income and let
the contractor speculate on prices, yields, and marketing costs. On these
scores alone, many growers have no alternative but to rely on the "pre-harvest
contractors," but the most compelling reason for resorting to this channel
has been the need for cash.
21.       The magnitude of the income foregone by the growers selling their
fruits to "pre-harvest contractors" instead of directly consigning to
comrmision merchants is very large. It is estimated that the farmgate price
realized by the growers selling to the "pre-harvest contractors" is about
15 to 20% less than it would be if the fruit were directly consigned to
the commission agents.



ANNEX 4
Page 7
Forwarding Agents
22.        All the fruit consigned to commission agents is taken over at
roadheads by a "forwarding agent" who makes the necessary transportation
arrangements. He charges a fixed fee of about Rs 0.25 per 18 kg case of
apples, and is usually a local shopkeeper or a well-established grower pro-
viding this service on a part-time basis during the season. It is estimated
that there are about 750 forwarding agents in HP. They handle the entire
trade of about five million cases of apples each season, so that the esti-
mated total of the forwarding charges is Rs 1,250,000. One agent on the
average handles about 7,000 cases and collects about Rs 1,700 per season.
The volume of the business handled varies widely, however, from one forward-
ing agent to another.
23.        It has been estimated that the top 20% of the agents handle about
70% of the entire crop, so that an average forwarding agent in the top
bracket handles about 23,000 cases, or 60 truckloads of the fruit, and col-
lects about Rs 6,000 per season. Considering that all he has to do is
assemble the apples and have the trucks come to transport them to their
final or intermediate destinations, and that there is little cash expense
involved, such business is undoubtedly very profitable. MIost of the fruits
are transshipped once or twice before reaching their consignees, and at
each stage of transsnipment another forwarding agent arranges the orward
transportation.  Normally the forwarding charges at the transshipment points
are higher than those in the interior, and the average is estimated to be
about Rs 0.30 per case for eacih transshipment.
Commission Agents and Wholesalers
24.        The commission agents in Delhi play the most critical role in
marketing apples in India. The bulk of Indian apple production is consigned
to them for distribution in Delhi as well as redispatch to other urban cen-
ters.  While their norrmnal role as cormission agents is a simple one of sell-
ing the fruits on behialf of the growers and other shippers for a fixed com-
mission fee of about 7Z of the wholesale value, rany of them simultaneously
act as speculative wholesalers (Table 3). They buy, on their own account
or that of their affiliates, apples on the market and sometimes those
consigned to them. They decide, witn or witiiout the coi±sernt or even
knowledge of the consignors, to put the fruits into cold storages for speculative
purposes.  In fact, it is so difficult to distinguish between what they
do in the capacity of commission agents from what they do as speculative
whlolesalers that they are often referred to as "counission1 agent-cum-
wholesaler."
25.       Tihere are about 500 commission agents in Dellhi dealing with
fruits and vegetables.  About 140 of themn handle apples, and only 40 of
these nandle nothing but apples.  These 40 cozmmission agents, special-
izing in apples, handle the lion's share of about 70% of the total volume
of the top ten conmission agents who handle over 50% of the apples in
Delhi. They sell the fruits to buyers of five different categories: (i)
fellow comrmission agents in the capacity of whiolesalers, (ii) full-fledged



ANINEX 4
Page 8
local wholesalers, who are not commission agents, (iii) outstation whole-
salers, (iv) Mashakhoras (sub-wholesalers), and (v) a limited number of
large local retailers. The full-fledged wholesalers outnumber the commission
agents, and there are about 250 Mashakhoras operating in the Delhi market.
26.       About 80% of the apples coming to the Delhi market are redispatched
to other urban markets, and the consumption within the greater Delhi area
has been estimated to be less than 20% of the total arrivals. Although some
of the apple shipments to outstations are undertaken by the outstation whole-
salers who participate in the Delhi market auctions and buy the fruits for
themselves, the redispatch to outstations is normally done by the Delhi
commission agents and wholesalers. They do it either: for consignment
sale of their own fruits at their own risk through outstation commission
agents who charge commission fees ranging from 7 to 8%; or for delivery
to the outstation comnission agents on whose behalf they buy the fruits for
a brokerage fee of 2 to 3.12% of the purchase price called "Bijak", all the
risk being borne by the outstation merchants. For the consignment sale,
tne Delhi mercliants are not paid advances except the varying and limited
amounts of downpayment from exceptionally eager outstation commission
agents, but the delivery unaer the "Bijak" system nearly always involves
advance payment of the full amount including the "Bijak'.
27.       Except for the portion that is disposed of by outright selling
or is bought by the commission agents themselves, the apples are marketed
in Delhi and all other major terminal markets through two types of auction:
open bidding and what is known as "Hatha" system, the latter being the normal
procedure. The fruits, in lots of varying size ranging from 10 to 200 cases
of 18 kg and averaging about 40, are displayed and, in the absence of quality
control, the prospective buyers invariably inspect the content of individual
boxes before offering prices. In the open bidding, the buyers call out their
bids until there is no higher bid forticoming, and the lot is sold to the high-
est bidder. In the niatha system, which has given rise to many controversies,
the commission agent offers a price for the lot to each buyer by manipulating
his hands and fingers with those of the bidders under cover of a cloth, and
in this esoteric hand-and-finger language the bargaiiiing is carried out. He
goes around and repeats the process with all the interested participants;
no one gets to know what othlers have offered, not even whether the buyer is
in fact the higliest bidder. As tlhe transaction is shrouded in secrecy, most
of the growers regard the whole Iiatha system with apprehension and hostility.
2s.       In addition to selling tiie fruits, the commission agents carry
out other important activities. In order to assure the supplies, they
advance r.oney to their steady consignors. The advance seldo,m exceeds
Rs 6.00 per case of apples they expect to handle, and is little more than
the packing cost of the fruit, but it is usually for the most critical cash
requirements of the growers. They advance tie transportation cost, forwarding
charges, taxes, and all otlher incidentals en route. Thliey maintain the
marlket facilities. They provide suppliers' credits to the wholesalers
and sub-iwholesalers.  They purc.hase fruits on orders from outstation traders
and for their own speculative purposes.



ANNEX 4
Page 9
29.       Many of the growers, especially the srmiall ones, are hostile to the
commission agents. They assert that the commission rates are too high,
that the proceeds are remitted to them often after long and undue delays,
and that the commission agents cheat on the prices at which the fruits are
sold. At present the commission rate is 7% of the wholesale value, and the
commission agents pay rebates, called "Vapsi", of 2% of the remittance
(equivalent to 2% of the packed fruits f.o.b. at farmgate) to those growers
who do not use the advances. Theoretically, the effective rate, net of
Vapsi, is therefore 5 to 6% of the wholesale value but, since most of the
growers actually take advances, the effective average rate is not much less
than 7X. A long time has elapsed since the present rate came into force,
and as the quantities and prices of apples have greatly increased in the
meantime, the commission agents must be earning much more and may be willing
to accept a lower rate if systematically challenged. In the absence of any
organized bargaining on tile part of the growers, however, there is little
chance that the rate will climb down.  There are many instances of frustrating
delays in remittance of the proceeds after the realization. It is not in-
herent in the system, and the solution to this problem must be sought in
exercising good judgement in the choice of cormmission agents. As regards
the cheating, the system of consignment sale where the consignees may choose
to buv on thleir own accounts inevitably carries with it wide scope for mal-
practices. It would require very high ethical standards of conduct on the
part of thie consignees to be always fair.  Moreover, the very esoteric nature
of the hatlia system is bound to give rise to serious allegations.
30.       The volume of the business and the earnings of the commission
agents in Delhi are very large.  They have been estimated to handle near-
ly the entire apple exports of Jammu and Kashnir and over 60% of that of
iP, so that the physical volume of their business would have been about
146,000 tons, or an equivalent of 8 million cases of 18 kg in 1970/71. As-
suming, conservatively, an average wholesale price of Rs 31.00 per 18 kg
case, which was the average realized for -uP apples for the year, the total
turnover works out at about Rs 248 million.  Assuming, conservatively again,
an average commission rate of 6% net of the Vapsi rebate, the total revenue
from commission charges amounts to about Rs 15 million, or an average of
Rs 750,000 for each of the top ten who handle 50% of the business, and
Rs 260,000 for each of the top 40.  In addition, most of the commission
agents have incomes of widely varying amounts from speculative activities
and procurement under the Bijak system.
31.       W;hile the conmission agents make very large financial outlays,
they are mostly in thie form of advances for packing, transportation, cold
storage charges, tax levies, postages, etc. wihich are reimbursed upon
realization of the proceeds, and tney entail no major cost.  The typical
commission agent keeps a shiop costing hlim about Rs 100,000 at most to acquire,
a wareihouse costing him about iUs 500 per month in rent, a car or a jeep,
a telephone, and a few experienced employees.  On the whole, thie trade is
undoubtedly very profitable.



AiNEX 4
Page 10
'lashakhoras (Sub-whiolesalers)
32.       Although some portion of the apples destined for local consumption
pass from the whiolesalers directly to the final retail outlets, most of
them pass to Mashakhoras who in turn distribute them to the retailers.  Some
Mashakhoras are engaged in shipping the fruits to other markets, but their
main business is local distribution. There are about 250 i'Jashakhoras
operating in Delhi and the business is heavily concentrated in the hands of
top 25 .Nashakhoras who pre-empt over 50% of the fruits passing through the
sub-wholesale stage.  In terrs of number, they are the dominant group of
jiduers at auction.  Their business is to buy the fruits at the wholesale
market and distribute them with a relatively small margin of about 2/.
Eike size of the lots they buy normally ranges frorm 4 to 40 18 kg cases, but
it can be as large as 50 and as small as a single case.
33.        Thle Mlashakhoras perform specific functions of four different
categories in the trade. First, based on tlhe detailed information on the
retail side, they speculate on moment-to-moment and day-to-day prices and
play an important role in the short-run price formation.  Second, on the
strengtii of their proven dependability, they procure the fruits on credit
of 10 to 15 days from a limited number of their patron commission agents
and wholesalers, and deliver them to their client retailers on credit of,
normally, two to seven days.  Third, they enable tne retailers to procure
tle fruits in smiall lots of desired assortment of varieties and size-grades.
Fourthi, tihey arrange the short-haul transportation of the fruits from the
market to the retailers.
Retailers
34.        There are about 8,000 retail outlets in Delhi.  Five thousand of
tiuem are fruit stalls arin they hiandle about 30,- of the retail trade.  The
remaining 20U%o is handled by the hawkers in the season nuibering about
3,000.  Most of these retailers procure the fruits from %ashakhoras on
credit of two to seven days on a revolviing basis, and are aligned witli
thaeir particular patron Mashallioras.  Their averae gross margiin is estimated
to be about 252 of the sub-wholesale value.  Thie estimated average volume
of business in Delhi is about two cases per stall and one case per hawker
per day during a 120-day season.  Tihe struCture of the retail trade is
very much tile sane in every major uroan market.

thce entire interstate sunplies arrive there for local consumption or re-
dispatcih to smaller towns sturrounding them.  The data available on the
arrivals in eaci of these major markets are very incomplete and account for
onlv about 3.) of thie estimated total supply for 197t0/71, but they provide

36.       Since the data on the apple exports from the producing states are
fairly accurate, the total arrivals for each of these markets can be estimated.
Botli Jammu and Kashmir and LiP keep close tabs on their apple exports, and the
data so collected indicate that for 1970/71 tile apple production and exports
were about 157,000 and 110,000 tons respectively for Jamrmu and Kashmir, and
76,000 and 61,000 tons for HP. The Uttar I''-adesh data are less satisfactory,
but the state exported about 50% of its estimated production of 40,000 tons

38.       Due to laclk of precision in the collection and specification
of the prices and of the data o0i tLhe arrivals and the volume of transactions,
very limited use can be made of the data for analytic purposes. For example,
the series simply give a price ran-c for Particular varieties of apples
on a weekly basis without any reference to rrade, quality, premiurms and
discounts, or the volumc of transactions.  The fact that the data are avail-
able only for a three-year period also precludes any useful consideration
of price trends, particularly as the 1970/71 season had an exceptionally
poor crop, followed bv another unfortunate season for 1971/72 with hail-
storms, transport disruptions and alternate gluts and shortages.  rurther-
more, 1972/73 was a-ain anotiier exception)al year for the industry; due to the
hailstorms immediately following pollinatior., the crop was a very poor one
of only about 30,7 of the expected normal productioni.  The general impression
is, hiowever, that over the past years, prices have fallen somewhat as
increased supplies have come onto the markret, and that the average price for
iP apples fluctuatec around Rs 2.00 per k7.
39.       Thne seasonal pattern of prices follows the normal trend of rela-
tively highi levels at the beginning- of tne season followed by declines



XNNEX 4
Page 12
as heavy supplies reach the market.  This is followed by some mild recovery
until tihe end of the lharvest.  During the off-season, prices for cold storage
apples climb steadily and, towards the end of the storage life, relatively
small consignments bring unusually high prices. Todate, the full potential
of the off-season market in the main markets lhas not been exploited.
40.       There are a few consistent features in the price structure:  (i)
disportionately large premiums are paid for the top quality apples; (ii) much
higher prices are paid for red apples; (iii) Royal Delicious, Red Delicious
from IIP and Nahraji from Jammu and Kashmir are the most popular varieties;
(iv) Golden Delicious is little sought after; (v) apples of the large grade
of a given variety of comparable quality fetcn about Rs 5.00 more per case
of 18 kg than medium grade, while those of small grade fetch about Rs 5.00
less; (vi) apples arriving in large lots of say, 100 cases or more, packed
in hiigh quality packing materials witlh consistency (quality control) in
contents fetcn about Rs 5.00 more per case than comparable apples packed
with poor packing ma ,rials arriving in small lots; (vii) even with the most
conservative estirnzae of off-season marketing cost of Rs .60 per kg, (carry-
ing cost plus cold storage and conunission charges), reasonable quality
after storaye hlas always assured lucrative returns; (viii) due to lack of
coordination of shipment among the shippers, whose choice of market for
consignment is determineu atomistically, there is a tendency for prices,
at Bombay in particular, to show the cobweb effect, that is, a year
with high prices relative to Delhi alternating with a year with low prices
resulting from relatively large consignments attracted by the high prices
of thte previous year and so on, and (ix) the elasticity of the demand is,
as is implicit in the above, a definitely limited one.

for HP apples at each major market was deduced fror, thle average farm-ate
value and the marketing costs associated with the various markets.  Tne
estimates so made are Aiven in Table 6.  These estimates have been tested
