ANNEX 5
Page 1
INDIA
HIMACHAL PRADESH APPLE PROCESSING
AND MARKETING PROJECT
Project Packing, Cold Storage, Processing and
Forwarding Facilities
Location Analysis
1.        General.  Apple plantings are widely dispersed over mountainous
Himachal Pradesh. While many newer plantings have no direct road access but
are on scattered small slopes, there are several areas with fairly concen-
trated plantings which have developed, on relatively large slopes and level
terrain, into communities served by a road. The few such communities account
for about 60% of present and projected production and were therefore selected
as the most appropriate areas to introduce the large-scale, mechanized pack-
ing operations provided by the project. Present and projected apple produc-
tion for these communities, shown in Table 1, is within a six to seven mile
circular radius of the community centers. The community centers - and in
the case of large communities, other major roadheads - would serve as sites
for packing houses and apple collection stations.
2.        Based on the production projections of the selected communities
and the existing road network, an investment program for apple packing houses
and collection stations has been prepared (Table 2). The basic assumptions
are that producers with 30% to 45% of the fruit (1978 projections) in the
catchment areas would be willing to utilize the packing services at the
projected unit cost. The unit service costs are based on the projected invest-
ment and operating costs (Annex 11, Table 1). As described in Annex 11,
the packing houses and collection stations would, in financial terms, serve
three operations: (a) apple purchases, (b) apple marketing as service, and
(c) apple packing as service. To prevent substantial damage to apples and
to ensure passage during the rainy harvest season, all roads linking the
packing and collection house system with the state and national road network
would be bituminized (Annex 6, para 2). To attract more produce to the
project facilities and to help relieve the growing transport problems, about
eight areas of heavy orchard density in some packing house and collection
station supply areas would be opened through installation of cableways
(Annex 6).
3.        Packing Houses.  The investment program calls for nine packing
houses stations with rated annual capacities of 5,000 tons, one with 4,000
tons and two with 3,000 tons. The capacities were determined on the basis
that: (a) 80% of the fruit would flow during a 30 day period and the other



ANNEX 5
Page 2
20% would be packed in an additional 20 days; and (b) an average working day
would consist of 10 hours. There may be periods when fruit flow would be
more heavily concentrated but the temporary storage of over-flow fruit was
considered more economical than the planning of larger capacity packing
houses.
4.        Collection Stations.  The three collection stations included in
the project are all rated at 5,000 tons per season, although their actual
capacities could be much greater, since their basic service is the
protection of fruit from the weather until trucking services can be provided
to take it to a packing house or to its final destination. Therefore, the
daily flow of fruit through a collection station can be large, depending on
the development of apple production and availability of trucking services.
These collection stations can also be used as warehouses during the off-season,
which would lower overhead costs. The collection stations are planned in
the areas of Chichiot, Manali and Rohru, but projected fruit production in
these three areas is insufficient to justify their conversion to packing
houses before 1978. They are planned so that they can be used as labor-
intensive packing houses during seasons with abnormally large production
and converted to regular packing houses when total production in their
respective areas becomes sufficient to warrant the installation of mechanical
packing equipment.
5.        Transshipment Center (Haryana/Delhi Border).  As an integral com-
ponent of the marketing chain, the project would also include the construc-
tion of a transshipment center at the Haryana/Delhi border. The need for
such a center arises from: (a) transport regulations restricting inter-state
movement of trucks; (b) the greater opportunity for obtaining backloads in
the Delhi area, coupled with the reluctance of truck owners to widen their
areas of operation because of scarcity of backloads; (c) the collection of
Himachal Pradesh apples from exit points not linked with the existing trans-
shipment center at Parwanu; and (d) the spoilage of fruit which now occurs
because of inadequate facilities at this point. The transshipment center
would attract a substantial proportion of the produce which now passes through
the intermediate Delhi market for ultimate disposal in Bombay, Calcutta and
distant markets. This proportion has been estimated at about 20,000 tons of
apples, calling for an installed storage capacity of 600 tons in the new
transshipment center.
6.        Cold Storage.  The project would also include construction of four
units of 2,000 tons each, three of them in Delhi and the other in Calcutta.
Several factors influenced the decision to site them in market areas, chiefly:
(i) they would act as reservoirs where apples to be sold during harvest
season could be stored to permit more sensitive responses to fluctuations
in the wholesale markets; (ii) they could be used for products other than
apples when there were no apples to store. These four units would complement
two similar units under construction in Delhi and Bombay.  Two of the four
project units would be constructed in the second project year and the other
two in the third (Chart WB-7320(3R). Thus, the marketing unit would have
12,000 tons of storage capacity for the 1976 harvest.



ANNEX 5
Page 3
7.        Processing Plant.  There is no commercial processing of apples in
Himachal Pradesh. Consequently, at least 20% of the annual crop--the culls--
is wasted. In years of hail damage, losses can exceed 50% of the crop in some
areas. The demand for apples in processed forms, mainly apple juice, appears
to be relatively elastic, but the high costs of shipping supplies and
bottles from the plains to the HP area make it almost impossible to produce
apple juice and market the product in consumer size containers. However,
these costs can be greatly reduced by producing concentrates at a central
point where all the packing house culls can be centralized and then shipping
the concentrates to consumer centers for bottling in consumer size units.
The precise location of the processing plant would be determined in the
first year of project execution.
VTechnical Description
8.        General.  The technical descriptions in the following paragraphs
provide only basic information on building and equipment requirements and
on the product flow through the project facilities. As outlined in Annex 8,
detailed designs and specifications would be drawn up for each type of
facility, e.g. for the nine 5,000-ton, the one 4,000-ton and the two
3,000-ton packing houses, the three collection stations, four cold storage
units, and the processing plant.
9.        Design of Packing Houses and Collection Stations.  All packing
houses and collection stations would be steel-framed buildings with a clear
span of about 20 m, a length of 75 m and an eaves height of 4 m (Chart
WB-7267).  They would have a covered area of 1,500 m2, a flat
concrete floor, hollow concrete block walls, an insulated asbestos roof, and
elevated ramps at the reception and shipping areas to enable the use of
pallets and hand2trolleys. The minimum site area required for the building
would be 4,000 m , but it is desirable to acquire one ha to allow for
emergency backlog storage space and future expansion. This upper limit
has been used in the investment cost estimates (Annex 9, Table 2).
10.       Packing House Equipment.  The equipment for the packing houses
would consist mainly of the following groups: (a) grading equipment, (b)
packing and handling equipment, (c) a jeep for the manager, and (d) a stand-
by generator of 15 kw capacity. Each packing house would have two complete
grading lines composed as follows:
(a) for the 5,000-ton houses: 48 in feed belt, four lane
sizers, six rotary packing tables (4 mi each in diameter), a
sorting table, an electric motor (three phase 220-240 volts),
and an overhead belt for "C" grade apples.
(b) for the 3,000 and 4,000-ton houses: 36 in feed belt, three
lane sizers, six rotary packino tables of 1.8 m diameter each,
a sorting table, an electric motor (three phase 220-240 volts)
and an overhead belt for "C"' grade apples.



ANNEX 5
Page 4
The packing and handling equipment would consist of 800 wooden pallets for
the 5,000-ton packing house (660 for the 4,000-ton and 480 for the 3,000-ton
packing houses), some hand trolleys (16 for 5,000-ton, 14 for 4,000-ton and
12 for 3,000-ton packing houses), and lidding and labeling stands (seven for
5,000-ton, six for 4,000-ton and five for 3,000-ton packing houses).
11.       Fruit Reception.  The fruit would be received at roadheads or pack-
ing houses packed in jumble or loosely in boxes. From the roadheads, trucks
would bring apples to the packing houses on wooden pallets measuring 1 m x
1 m with four-way entry. Each pallet would hold 30 boxes (six boxes in one
layer x five high). Trucks arriving at the packing houses would back up,
tailgates down, to the unloading ramp. To facilitate unloading and to compen-
sate for possible height differences between the truck and the unloading
ramp, an iron plate would be placed to bridge the ramp and truck so that
it would be possible to board the truck with a hand trolley and transport
the pallet load of boxes to the reception and pre-grading area. The same
system would be used when loading the trucks for shipment if the fruit was
packed. If the destination were to be a transshipment center, the fruit
could be shipped on pallets. If the fruit were being sent to market or
cold store at a distant point, it would travel packed in boxes but unpalletized,
and the pallets would only be used to facilitate the loading process. The
storage area would be for approximatelv 250 tons.
12.       In seasons of heavy crops, or during certain peak periods of a
normal year, there might be a backlog of packed or unpacked fruit because
of possible road blockages or because of heavy pickings in a short period of
time. The total amount of packed and unpacked fruit which could conceivably
be accumulated at a packing house might reach 600 tons at any one time.
If over 250 tons of storage were frequently required, a light structure would
be built adjacent to the packing house. The packing house roof could be
extended by the distance indicated in the layout in order to accommodate a
possible backlog of apSles. This framed unit with a 60 x 15 m pitch roof,
would give about 900 m storage space. This space could accommodate over
300 tons of fruit. The roof would be corrugated asbestos with a minimum
height of three m at the rear and five m in front and extended for about
four m at the front. The side and rear walls could consist of woven matting.
This sort of structure could be constructed at a maximum cost of Rs 100 per
i2.
m.
13.       Packing Line.  (See also Appendix 1).  From the unloading ramp the
apples (in cases or jumble packed) would be channeled to the reception and
pre-grading area. Wherever conditions permit, the fruit should be shipped
in open unlidded cases. Where this is not practical, the lids would be
put on the boxes with a minimum of nails and with no strapping wires. The
fruit would be dumped by hand, gently, upon a rubber conveyor belt which
would feed onto a roller sorting table. A belt would run above this table
to convey the culls to a collecting bin where it would be jumble packed into
unlidded cases, put onto pallets and sent to the projected processing plant.
The remainder of the fruit would slowly pass over the revolving rollers,
to each side of which would stand the graders whose function would be to
pick out additional culls which may be badly bruised or hail-damaged fruit
or any other fruit which would not meet the desired marketing standards.



ANNEX 5
Page 5
There would usually be six to eight graders depending upon fruit quality.
If the fruit was of very high quality, four graders might be sufficient but
more might be necessary for low quality fruit.
14.       From the grading table the fruit would travel on a sizer consist-
ing of three or four lanes of parallel belts in a V formation. Revolving
rubber rollers, situated at various heights above the fruit transporting
belts, would finally eject the apples onto a belt which would transport the
fruit onto a revolving packing table. The packers would stand around the
packing tables and each would pack one quality of fruit, i.e., one or more
packers would choose the most high colored apples which would be "A" grade
while other packers on the rotary revolving table would take care of the
"B" fruits.
15.       Further Handling in Packing Houses.  After packing, including
wrapping, the full boxes would be placed on vallets and transported to an
area where the boxes would be lidded, strapped and stencilled. A problem
which would certainly arise is that of partially filled cases after running
through a lot of fruit from one grower. Among the possible solutions for
this problem would be purchase of this fruit by APMIC, or having the packing
station supervisor estimate the amounts of fruit of varying sizes and
grades, or some other equitable system which should eliminate as much as
possible stoppages in the packing line because of changing lots of fruit
from one grower to another. In the case of cooperative marketing, the
grower would be credited with the fruit which remained and the packing line
would continue to function. After the fruit would be palletized, it would
be transferred to the shipping area and await transport to a cold store or
market.
16.       Cold Stores.  (See Chart WB-7266) The four cold stores would be
multi-purpose units of sufficient refrigeration capacity to achieve a
rapid pulldown of fruit temperature after receipt of the fruit into the
cold store and with sufficient evaporative surface on the coolers to ensure
high humidity in the rooms. The basic specifications desired for apple
storage are: (a) sufficient cooling equipment to bring the fruit flesh
temperature to -10C to 0°C within seven days; (b) air velocities of not
less than 75 linear ft per minute for each fruit container. Usually one
cubic ft of air per box is ample blower capacity. After all the fruit in
the room is cooled down, this rate can be reduced by 1/3 - 1/2 (c) ample
relative humidity, that is, 90% to 92% after fruit flesh temperature has
reached O'C; and (d) an efficient defrosting svstem to keep the coils free
of ice.  There are various ways of doing this, such as periodically washing
the coils with fresh warm water from the condenser, using hot gas, or by
heating the units electrically.
17.       In the multi-purpose cold stores, cold air would be distributed
throughout the storage room by air ducts with openings every 1.5 m.
to ensure even air distribution. The fruit should be so stacked that there
is a space of 5 cm between rows. A space of 20 cm should be left along the
side walls of the room and a similar space should be left behind the boxes
and the rear walls opposite the air ducts. The sPaces between the rows



ANNEX 5
Page 6
should be kept open and in the direction of the air flow. Care should be
taken that the spaces be extended the entire length of the room in order to
ensure adequate circulation of cold air between and around the boxes of
fruit. The first fruit brought into the room should be stacked near the
openings of the air ducts so so as not to be heated by later fruit. Great
care must be taken not to block the air duct openings with boxes of fruit.
18.       Reliable thermometers should be hung in various locations in the
storage room to ensure even temperature controls which would then be evi-
dence of the air circulation conditions within the rooms. The thermometers
should be inspected and the temperatures noted at least twice a day. A
hair type hygrometer should also be placed in the storage room to ascertain
the relative humidity. However, hygrometers at 0°C temperatures are not
very accurate and not too much reliance should be placed on their readings.
The only method of ensuring sufficient relative humidity is reliable plan-
ning of the entire cold storage installation.
19.       During the fruit reception period several cases from each lot
would be opened and inspected by the person responsible to the marketing
authority; the condition of each sample and the location of the fruit in
the cold store would be noted in a log book. Efforts should be made to
segregate the fruit into various rooms according to condition at time of
reception. In this fashion, each room would contain fruit of similar stand-
ard and the fruit condition would then dictate market strategy and would
permit the marketing of the fruit in optimal condition. In event of unsuit-
able fruit being received for storage, the owner of the fruit should be
notified at once. The fruit should be set apart until the owner's direction
for disposal of the fruit was received. If the owner insisted on storing the
fruit for any time, he should be advised that this would be done entirely
at his own risk. Keeping an accurate log and inspecting fruit at time of
reception has often protected the cold store operators against controversy
with the fruit shippers, and has also been an effective device in assisting
the grower in his marketing operation.
20.       The cold stores would be basically used for apple storage between
late August and early March. It is assumed that some fruit would start
moving out from January onward. As soon as a room started emptying other
commodities could be stored. From the beginning of March until the end of
July potatoes would be stored; during August the cold stores would be closed
and undergo their annual maintenance.
21.       Transshipment Center.  (Chart WB-7288(R))  Within the
temperature range of 25°C to 35°C experienced at Delhi during July/September,
a fully enclosed building with fan-ventilation but without air conditioning
would have only marginal temperature advantages over a covered shelter with
ample natural ventilation. Therefore, a simple shelter which will protect
the apples from direct sunlight and rain and with easy access for the
simultaneous loading and unloading of a number of trucks is proposed.
Storage for 600 tons (37,500 cases) would be under two parallel open-fronted
sheds on either side of a central service road (see layout sketch chart).
This would be supported by office facilities and a truck-parking area on



ANNEX 5
Page 7
a 1/2 ha site surrounded by a security fence with a further 1-1/2 ha acres
acquired for expansion. It is proposed that each storage unit be a
steel-framed structure with a mono-pitch roof. The dimensions would be
60 m x 16 m with a minimum eaves height of 3 m at the rear and about 5 m at
the front. The roof would be of corrugated asbestos with a layer of insula-
tion material beneath. The roof would be extended by about 4 m at the front
to provide a covered loading and unloading canopy and by about 1 m at the
rear for weather protection. Hollow block concrete rear and side walls to
a height of 2 m with a 1 m air gap above are proposed. The whole storage
area and central roadway would have a well-drained concrete base, electric
lighting and a water supply. A series of standpipes and hoses in the storage
building would allow the concrete floor and surroundings to be hosed down
to assist cooling in extreme heat conditions.
22.       Juice Plant Design.  Subject to detailed engineering work,
an apple juice concentrate plant of about 12,000 tons of input a year would
be financed under the project. The plant would be designed and equipped
so that other fruits produced in HP, for instance plums, peaches and pears,
could be processed there. A site of 8,000 to 10,000 m2 would be required
on which the following buildings would be constructed:
(a)  a warehouse of 300 m :
2
(b) a production area of 250 m 2
(c) a boiler room of 150 m 2
2
(d) a procurement area of 850 mi 
2
(e) offices and a laboratory of 350 m.
23.       Processing Plant Equipment.  Appendix 2 lists in detail the
processing equipment needed for the plant. Major components of this multi-
fruit processing plant would be:
(a) a pressing station for apples, pears, stone fruits, grapes
and berries:
(b) an essence recoverv plant;
(c) an evaporator combined with desulphurization plant;
(d) a precoat filter;
(e) various tanks; and
(f) a complete laboratory.
24.       Process Description (See also Appendix 2 and Charts WB-7264 and
VB-7265).



ANNEX 5
Page 8
(a) Reception. The fruit would arrive at the procurement area in
boxes on wooden pallets. The pallets would be off-loaded from
the truck with the help of hand trolleys, and would be arranged
in the storage area. In case of very great quantities of fruit
arriving in a short period, the surplus fruit would be stored
in the open yard and covered with tarpaulin.
(b) Washing and Pressing. The fruit for processing would be dumped
into the soaker-washer where dirt and spray residues would be
removed and would then pass under nozzles where final cleaning
would take place. From there the fruit would pass over an
inspection table where rotten fruits would be discarded and the
clean fruit would go into the mills where it would be crushed.
The crushed material would be put into pressing racks between
cloths, in layers, and pressed hydraulically. While the fruit
would be pressed in the first press, the second press would be
prepared while the third press would be discharged.
(c) Poriace Disposal. The pressed pomace would be conveyed to the
pomace dryer and would be removed after it is dried to an 8%
moisture content. The dry material would then be put into sacks
and sold for cattle feed.
(d) Juice Pasteurization and Aromna Recovery. The raw juice (70%
vield, 100 Brix) which would come from the presses would pass
through a rotarv screen in order to remove coarse particles
which might have remained and the strained juice would be pas-
teurized at a temperature of 80'C. The pasteurized juice would
be then fed into a stripper where about 10% of the juice would
evaporate under atmospheric pressure. This vapor would contain
practically all the volatile essences. The vapor would then be
reduced to 10% of its original volume by fractional distillation
(7 kg aroma yield per ton of fresh apples). The aroma would then
be stored in cold wall tanks until needed.
(e) Juice Clarification, Concentration and Storage. The stripped
juice would he collected in settling tanks where it would
remain overnight.  During this period enzymes (pectinol) would
be added in order to decompose pectic substances and aid in
clarification. After the depectination process, a filter aid
(diomaecous earth) would be added and the mixture would be
filtered in the filter press. One ton of clarified clear
juice would then be concentrated under vacuum into approximately
150 kg concentrate of 720 Brix. The concentrate would then be
collected in cold wall tanks where it would be cooled and the
aroma would be returned to the juice. The juice would then be
pumped into tanks which would be kept in cold storage at a
temperature of 2°C.



ANNEX 5
Page 9
(f)  Shipping.  The concentrated juice would be shipped to soft
drink bottler-distributors in large consumer areas such as
Delhi. The juice could be transported in 20 kg containers.
The concentrate would then be reconstituted and bottled in the
consumer area.
(g)  Bottling.  A small bottling plant would also be utilized at
the processing plant in order to satisfy the needs of the local
market.
Operating Costs and Staffing Requirements
25.       Packing Houses.  Table 3 gives a breakdown of staff requirements
and costs for model packing houses of the three capacity types proposed
for the project. Most of the unskilled and semi-skilled laborers would be
employed up to three months a year. The maximum activity period needed
would be for the managers who would work for six months for the packing
house. Table 4 lists all the operating costs calculated for the three model
packing houses. Boxes and other packing material account for most of the
operating cost, exceeding Rs 1,000,000 in the case of the 5,000-ton
packing house, while the share of repairs and maintenance of structures
and equipment is virtually insignificant. The packing cost per box differs
only slightly for varving packing house sizes, since as can be seen in
Annex 11, Table 1, there are no significant economies of scale, even if
depreciation of structures and equipment, and working capital requirements
are taken into account.
26.       In addition to the assumptions in the footnotes of Table 4, the
operating costs for the model packing houses were calculated on the
following bases:
(a)  Percentage of Cullage:  Estimated at 20% of inDut.
(b)  Length of Harvest Period:  Approximiately 50 days.  However, it
is estimated that 80% of the fruit would be harvested in 39 days
in any one orchard.
(c)  Quantities:  A 5,000-ton per season installation would hiave to
be prepared to handle 4,000 tons in 30 days which (minus 20%
cullage) would be 3,200 tons in 30 days and a daily average of
110 tons or 11 tons per hour of a 10 hour day. The relevant
figures for a 4,000-ton capacity installation would be 85 tons
per day or 8.5 tons per hour of a 10 hour day.  The relevant
figures for a 3,000-ton capacity packing house would be 66 tons
per dav or 8 tons per hour of an 8-1/2 hour working day.
(d)  Equipment Output:  The equipment recommended for thlis project
consists of sizers of a belt and roller type of various number
of lanes, each lane sizing up to 2 tons per hour.  However,
because of probable stoppages due to changeovers from one



ANNEX 5
Page 10
grower's fruits to another's, it is envisaged that a four-
lane sizer would size approximately 1.75 tons per hour. For
this reason, four-lane sizers are recommended for the 5,000-ton
per season packing houses and three-lane sizers for the 3,000
and 4,000 tons per season packing houses.
(e)  Packing and Wrapping:  A nan would be able to wrap and pack at
least 80 cases per day. About 50 packers per dav would work
in the peak season and less before and after the peak.
(f) No. of Cases per Ton: It is assumed that 55 cases could be
packed from one ton of apples.
(g) No. of Cases per Pallet: The wooden pallets measuring 1 x 1 m
could hold 30 boxes of apples, i.e., a layer of six cases piled
five high.
27.       Collection Stations.  Although no apples would be processed in
the c:ollection stations, these structures would be important as temporary
storage points and integral parts of the project. As can be seen from
Table 5, most of the operating cost would consist of staff remuneration.
The manager and two watchmen would be working for the collection stations
full time, as it is anticipated that they would become storage and trading
pcints for other agricultural inputs.
28.       Cold Storage.  Large differentials between harvest and post-harvest
apple prices provide a very attractive incentive for building and operating
the four additional cold storage units (Annex 4). The chance of using them
for other produce during several other months each year would further increase
the revenues from this part of the project. However, the operating costs
listed in Table 6 take into account only overations related to apple storage,
except for staff and insurance costs.
29.       Transshipment Center.  The annual throughput of the transshipment
center has been assumed to be about 20,000 tons, which would be handled
during the harvest season of about 100 days. On an average, the apples
would be stored for two days in the center. Table 7 shows that most of the
operating costs would be salaries and wages and would average Rs 14/ton.
30.       Apple Juice Plant.  Subject to feasibility and engineering studies,
tentative cost estimates for the proposed apple juice concentrate and essence
plant: have been prepared.  As shown in Table 8, the plant would require about
20 permanent emnloyees, some of whom would need considerable experience.
Very likely two or three of the specialists needed would have to be expatriates,
at least for one season. To spread the cost of permanent labor, considerable
efforts should be made to stretch the operating season by processing other
fruits and vegetables too. Among the operating costs, raw materials
constitute bv far the largest component (Ps 5.5 million). For further
details see Table 9.

INDIA
HIMACHAL PRADESH APPLE PROCESSING AND MARKETING PROJECT
Project Transport Improvement
Introduction
1.        The project calls for the improvement of existing roads and the
construction of new roads in the project production area to overcome the
increasingly difficult transport situation. In addition, aerial cableways
would be established in remote apple production areas where porters are not
available and roads not economically justified. The new roads constructed
under the project would fall into two categories: access and feeder. Access
roads would connect packing stations and feeder roads would connect orchards
to the state highway network.
2.        Some roads would allow for the expansion of existing apple growing
areas while other roads would induce development of new areas. Mbst roads
would induce incremental production of many agricultural commodities in
addition to their impact on apple production. Since there would be 12
packing houses and 3 collection stations and each would need to be serviced
by access and feeder roads to ensure a total supply of about 55,000 tons
of apples per annum to the packing houses, the road construction and
improvement program in Appendix 1 has been agreed at negotiations. The
road works required can be summarized as follows:
Access     Feeder
Roads      Roads       Total
------r--- -
New Construction                       14          83         97
Improvements
- Cross drainage, base and
surfacing                       -         267         267
-  Base and surfacing only            -         37          37
Total                       14         387         401
All roads over which apples travel would have to be bituminized to reduce
bruising of fruit. The project also calls for the supply of maintenance
equipment to keep the roads open during the harvest period.
Road Design Standards
3.        As apples are very susceptible to bruising, the roads, particu-
larly those over which apples would be transported in an unpacked state



ANNEX 6
Page 2
\  --S-.-i :-ear~ zo pae=n; r.c-ses). wou'ld have to be built with a high quality
r.LainS surface. Furt.rermore, as transport would take place largely during
tae :[onsoon saason, the surface should be sufficiently durable to resist
weather damage. Accordingly, the project roads would be built to the
following standards:
(a)  Access: to a formation width of 3.65 m and
pr3vided wih- a 2.45 m wide bitumen surface; they
will be widener  to 4.9 m formation with 3.05 m bitumen
surface on corners to permit passing; and
F Feeder:  to a formation width of 4.9 m with a
2.45 m wide bitumen surface.
4.        Because of the precipitous nature of the terrain, the roads would
be located along mountain sides and would be designed geometrically to follow
the contour of the hillsides. Radius of curvature would be reduced to an
absolute minimum so that the roads could follow the terrain and be located
entirely on cut. Detailed designs and cost estimates have been prepared by
P'WID.
Road Cost Estimates
5.        Costs per km of the various types of work which would be involved
range as follows:
(a)  Access Roads                             Rs/Km
(i)  new construction                50,000 to 300,000
(b) Feeder Roads
(i)  new construction (complete)  125,000 to 250,000
(ii)  cross drainage                  20,000 to  50,000
(iii); base and surfacing              45,000 to  50,000
Based on these estimates, total road investment cost would amount to
Rs 45.0 millio,-. For details on the road construction program, see Appendix
.  Since roa. maintenance coszs are presently estimated at about Rs 4,500
oer ann._n, the maintenance budget for project roads would total Rs 1.8
million ner anrnum.
Execcution of Road Ptowram.
6.        General.  The transportation component of the project would be
c-rried out by da:e    174 with detailed plans and specifications for the work.
-. the field, construc-:on works would be controlled by Works Divisions,
eac& under tnhe charge of an 'xecutive Engineer.  The supervision organization
woula be discussed at negotiations.



ANNEX 6
Page 3
7.        Construction.  The improvement of existing roads and construction
of new roads under the project would form part of the State's Road Develop-
ment Plan for the next four years, in which they would be accorded priority.
Due to a relatively short construction season (6 months), contractors have
been unwilling to undertake work in the hilly areas. The State therefore
executes about 70% of its roacl development by force account with manual labor.
Some local contractors undertake the balance; this system works statisfactorily.
Accordingly, it is recommended that this method be used to undertake the road
work necessary for the project.
8.        Maintenance.  The PWD would assume responsibility for maintaining
the roads to be improved or constructed under the project. To reduce land-
slide-caused transport delays, which can be as long as seven days, FWD would
need additional maintenance equipment of an approximate value of US$0.7
million (Annex 9, Table 8).
Cableways
9.        General.  To help relieve the increasing transportation problem
of farmers in remote areas in getting the apples from orchards to the
roadheads, the project would provide farmers with various cableways
of a total length of about 40 km. These would be the first cableways for
agricultural produce in HP. Locations for the cableways would be determined
where the transportation situation is particularly bad and becoming increasingly
difficult. If the project cableways prove effective, it is expected that
cableway installation would be extended to other areas. Calculations show
that the financial viability would be satisfactory. Various cableway designs
are available and the most suitable type would be selected on the basis of
suppliers submitting turnkey proposals. Although the cableways would be
primarily constructed for apple transport, efforts would be made to utilize
them in the apple off-season for other agricultural produce, mainly potatoes,
and also for getting inputs to the farmers.
10.       Equipment, Engineering and Construction.  The basic equipment
for the cableways would be steel cables, trestles, cradle cars, an electric
or combustion motor with a driving winch, and anchorings and holding devices.
A number of concrete loading platforms would be constructed along the cable-
ways so that growers could avoid uphill carrying of apples to the greatest
extent possible. Right-of-way would be only a minor problem, since (a) only
insignificant parts of land would be needed; and (b) the cableways would
cross mainly orchards from which they would carry apples. The tentative
requirements on the performance of one cableway would be: (a) length: two
to eight km; (b) gradient: 45° to 60°; (c) capacity: five to ten tons per
hour; and (d) speed: five to six km per hour. Bidders would present their
respective engineering studies and design proposals. Engineering and
execution would be closely supervised by BID.
11.       Operation.  As it is anticipated that the cableways operating
season would be increased beyond the apple harvest season and utilization
of capacity expanded, AIC would be running this part of the project as a
means of public commodity transportation. In Table 1, operating costs of a



ANNEX 6
Page 4
moe  cabieway are identified.  Major items among the operating cost would be
repairs and maintenance (nainly because of the high replacement cost for
cables), and labor. 7Te operators would be employed permanently. They would
also be responsible for routine maintenance and ensure that the cableways
would be useable all year round. In addition, two laborers would be employed
termanently. During the apple harvest season, an additional ten unskilled
laborers would work for the cableways.
7.2. ~ pFees. To keep transportation fees as low as possible, the utili-
Z'L_;-n per hour and per annum should be maximized. This could be mainly
acnie-d by two means:  (a) an individual cableway should connect as many
weil-b earing orchards as possible, and (b) most of the cableway catchment
area should be at least 3 km away from the next roadhead. To determine
appropriate users' fees and AIC rentals the mission projected a cash flow for
A:C cableway operations (incorporated in Annex 13) based on the following
operational assumptions: (a) the catchment area of one cableway would consist
of 1,000 ha (5 km length and supply areas of 1 km on both sides, for instance);
(b) the marketed apple production would be at least 5,000 tons in each catch-
ment area (for instance, 500 ha at an average yield of 10 tons); (c) the
average distance of the lift would be 3 km and, therefore, fees would be
collected for 15,000 km-tons; (d) utilization would increase to 20,000 km-tons
within five years. It was found that a breakeven operation would require
a pro-rated fee of about Rs 40 per km per ton. If only apples were transported
by the cableway, the minimum fee to be charged per case of apples (18 kg)
would be about Rs 0.80 per km. This rate and even higher ones would be
readily acceptable to apple growers in remote areas where transport of the
apples to the roadheads immediately after picking has become most critical.
This fee level would also be in line with the porters' remunerations in areas
where porters are still available. The pro-rated cableway fees might be
reduced to the extent other commodities would be transported.
13.       Maintenance Requirements.  Particularly during the first season,
a qualified maintenance/operation technician would have to be available
within 24 hours. The cableways supplier should be committed to provide this
service. For subsequent years, it should be ensured that for major repairs,
mechanical services would be available within two to three days, particularly
during the apple harvesting season. Finally, there would be routine visits
before and after the apple season by a qualified technician to ensure proper
maintenance, PWD would have trained a sufficient number of cableway
.echr.>izans by an appropriate organization.

IN%DIA
HIMACRAL PRADESH APPLE PROCESSING AND MARKETING PROJECT
Technical Assistance/Training Programs
Technical Assistance
1.        As the project is the first of its type in India and of a very
complex nature, considerable know-how has to be imported and transferred
to local staff to ensure successful project operation. In SDR a department
would be established with exclusive responsibility for technical assistance
and training. Two types of technical assistance would be financed under the
project: (a) activities of foreign consultants in India to assist in training
project staff and in design and initial operation of facilities; and (b)
training of project staff in foreign countries. Five-foreign consultants
would work for a total of 75 man-months in India. Twenty-one Indian staff
would undergo a totalftraining period of 99 man-months abroad.  Three foreign
hosts would make all necessary day-to-day arrangements for this overseas
training and, as much as useful, accompany the Indian trainees abroad to
maximize the training success. The total technical assistance cost is estimated
at Rs 3,940,000 (about US$493,000). The proposed technical assistance and
training program has been summarized in Appendices 1 and 2, and Chart WB-
7321(3R).
General Scope of Training
2.        Training would be given to both senior and junior project staff
in India and abroad. Senior staff would be trained mainly individually,
particularly abroad, while junior staff would undergo training in groups
of more than 10 participants. Local training would consist of two major
components, namely instruction courses with occasional field trips and
on-job training. Three types of training courses (and attendance at such
courses) would be financed under the project, namely, in: (a) apple
quality control; (b) apple packing plant, cold storage and sales management;
(c) sales promotion; and (d) cableways operation and maintenance. All these
courses would be held in India, some of them probably in Himachal Pradesh,
and would cost an estimated total of Rs 880,000 (Table 1). On-job training
would be given both during construction and the initial operation periods
of the project facilities. The foreign consultants would play a key role
in planning and establishing the training programs and also in training the
future senior project staff.
Training of Packing Houses and Cold Storage Staff
3.        General Packing Plant Supervisor.  There should be one general
supervisor of the packing plant managers. Minimum qualifications for the
position is a B.A. or B.S. degree in Agricultural Economics or Business



ANNEX 7
Page 2
Administration. He should have several years of management experience.
This general supervisor should have six months managerial training in
large apple packing companies, which should include several packing plants.
The trainee should be attached to management personnel; for applied training,
potential training sites are Sica Pomanjou in France, British Columbia Fruit
Growers in B.C., Canada, and Skookum Inc. at Wenatchee, Washington. General
managerial training could be further emphasized at Blue Goose Growers,
Sacramento, California.
4.        Agricultural Engineer.  Thirteen apple packing plant maintenance
supervisors are needed. One, who should be a trained agricultural engineer,
would be the chief technical supervisor for all packing plants. After
adequate training on packing house equipment, installation and maintenance
abroad, he would train the other 12 plant maintenance supervisors. He would
devote six months to working both at an equipment manufacture factory
assisting with installation of equipment and in an operating packing plant,
emphasizing maintenance of equipment being planned for use in India. A
foreign engineer should be supplied by the equipment company to supervise
and instruct during the installation of packing equipment, particularly for
the first line to be installed.
5.        Apple Quality Control Courses.  Two separate classes should be
conducted: one for 18 packing plant managers and 13 packing line superin-
tendents, and a second one for 13 grade and quality inspectors plus four
cold storage quality control superintendents. Basic training needs include
training in post-harvest fruit characteristics. This would be accomplished
with a three-month course using applied experience with fruit and measuring
instruments. General subjects of training would include: (a) maturity
and ripening processes of fruit - a subject which needs to be applied to
fruit from different producing areas and variable responses by year, variety
and exposure; (b) responses of fruit at different maturity levels to post-
harvest exposure to temperature, humidity, atmosphere; (c) post-harvest
fruit disorders and control measures; (d) training and experience with fruit
characteristic measuring instruments; and (e) economics of product standardi-
zation and quality control in fruit marketing.
6.        The courses should be conducted in the apple-producing area of
Himachal Pradesh, possibly near the Agricultural Research Station at
Mashobra and the University of Simla. Teaching staff could include staff
members of SDH, Mashobra Research Station and University. These personnel
would be assisted by the two foreign experts with experience in packing
plant management and apple quality control with emphasis on Red Delicious
and Golden Delicious apples (Appendix 1).
7.        Management Courses.  A course to increase the managerial skills
of 18 packing plant managers would include such subject matters as annual
and seasonal planning for packing house operation; fruit handling; labor
management; planning daily, weekly, monthly schedules; coordination of
fruit transport; maintaining a fruit quantity and quality log (record) for
each grower's fruit. The general packing plant supervisor (para 2) would,



ANNEX 7
Page 3
after two six-month training periods, one in india and one in a foreiga
apple industry area, supervise this course which is expected to last three
months.  The three cold storage managers would have to attend a three-month
general management course, preferably in India. Tne project entity would
have to select trainees and book -hem for suitable courses immediately
after the date of effectiveness.
8.        Field Training.  Technical skillls associated with harvest
maturity, measurement, grading, packaging, transporting and storing apples
would have to be developed. Tnis would be accomplished through field and
laboratory experience provided by periodic field trips and work periods
over five months from August through December. It would require full time
training during the first two months of apple harvest followed by four
market field trips in the months of November, December and January. General
subject matter for training would be the same as that covered in the course
on quality control. The difference would be the emphasis on using the
actual product and following it through the normal market channels. This
should be planned and conducted by that part of the staff which presented
the course and should also include the two foreign experts and SDA represen-
tatives.
Training of Sales Staff
9.        Sales Managers.  Practical training for six months abroad, and
attendance of a management course for three months, would constitute a minimum
training period and should be provided for the three managers. The recom-
mendation for practical training is based on the assumption that three well-
trained but inexperienced managers can be recruited. If their academic
training is weak, a much longer theoretical training program would be required.
A foreign consultant (Appendix 1) capable of organizing the training program
for managers would be particularly necessary to make the most effective use
of the foreign component of the managers' training program.
10.       Sales Promotion Training Program.  A training program for the 15
salesmen would be planned and conducted with considerable assistance from
the expatriate senior marketing expert. This training would use local
talent as instructors supplemented by occasional lectures from the senior
marketing expert. A six-month training period could be effectively used.
Training of Juice Concentrate Plant Staff
11.       Processing Engineer.  The processing plant engineer must be a
graduate food equipment processing engineer.  Tra_ning time would cover
one year of applied experience. It should begin with approximately six
months satellite work with a foreign equipment manufacturer. There should
be an opportunity to assist with installation of equipment in a pi-ant. A
six-month assignment should follow in an operating processing plant using
equipment like that to be used in the proposed plant.
12.       Food Technologists.  Two Indian fcod technologists should have a
minimum of M.S. in food technology and preferably also experience with food



ANNEX 7
Page 4
processing. Training shouic include.nine months' experience working in
deciduous fruit processing plants, which emphasize processed apple products.
Preferred location would be Western Europe. In India the food technologists
would also attend one of the two apple quality control courses (para 5).
13.       Processing P?ant Yanagers.  HPMC would hire and arrange training
for two processing plant managers. Minimum qualification should be a B.A. or
B.S. degree in Business Administration and a successful record of several
years' managemnent experience. The project would finance a minimum of
three months' theoretical training for the processing plant managers.
Addit. inal On-Job Training
14.       After having undergone the above training all trainees would
continue to be on the payroll of the project corporation. If the facilities
to which they would be assigned were not yet operating, they would be
assisting in and supervising construction of those facilities; supervising
installation of equipment; obtaining additional instruction during the test
run periods of the first facilities to operate; and assisting their colleagues
in charge of those facilities already operating. For training of cableways
technicians see Appendix 2 and Annex 6, paragraph 13.

HIMACHAL PRADESH APPLE PROCESSING AND MARKETING PROJECT
Engineering and Construction Procedures
1.        General.  As pointed out in Annex 11, due to the nature of the
project all activities would be integrated. The investment program has
been so designed that each project facility would be utilized to the greatest
extent possible from its very beginning. The phasing chart for the project
facilities (WB - 7320(4R)) shows, in detail, how the investment program
would be implemented, starting in late 1973 and terminating by the middle
of 1977. The packing houses would be the most critical facilities and
given highest priority, as improved grading and packing would be the basic
activities of the project. No engineering consultants would be needed for
the packing houses, collection stations and the transshipment center, since
construction would be of a fairly simple nature and the packing equipment of
a standard type. However, for the cold stores and the processing plant,
engineering consultants would have to be employed. It is anticipated that
engineering know-how would be available in India for cold stores, but that
for the processing plant foreign engineering consultants would have to be
hired. HPMC would be in charge of planning and developing the project
facilities. HP Government would give assurances that the SDH and PWD would
assist HPMC in doing this by providing the technical know-how needed. HP
Government would also help identify and acquire suitable construction land
in time. The two Appendices to this Annex identify the major engineering
activities anticipated. Paragraphs 2 to 5 describe the engineering and
construction procedures proposed for each group of facilities.
2.        Procedures for Packing Houses.  Based on the descriptions in
paragraphs 9 and 10 of Annex 5, HPMC would, immediately after credit effec-
tiveness, start site selection and acquisition procedures as well as the
specific engineering work for the first four packing houses. HPMC would use
the relevant data collected by SDH by that time (SDH started a survey of
the heaviest apple production areas in late 1972 to provide sufficient
information for a packing house location analysis). HPMC would draw on SDH's
and PWD's expertise as needed. In addition, an expatriate senior packing
plant manager (see Annex 7, Appendix 1) would assist HPMC in conducting the
planning and engineering work and preparing the tender documents for these
first packing houses. It would be a condition of credit effectiveness that
HPMC would have such an expert under contract. This expert would also,
together with SDH and PWD staff, assist HPMC in evaluating the bids for
construction and equipment. Construction and installation of equipment
would be supervised by HPMC staff, particularly the agricultural engineer
and competent PWD staff. In addition, the grading and packing equipment
supplier would provide an engineer to instruct and supervise during the
installation of equipment. Plans worked out and procedures followed for



ANNEX 8
Page 2
establishing the first four packing houses would be the basis for developing
the other eight packing houses.
3.        Procedures for Forwarding Facilities.  Engineering and construction
procedures for the three collection stations and the transshipment center
would be identical with those proposed for the packing houses with two
exceptions: (a) equipment would be a minor investment item, fairly simple
and locally procured; and (b) land acquisition for the transshipment center
at the Haryana/Delhi border might be more difficult, since the site would be
outside HP.
4.        Procedures for Cold Stores.  To accommodate some output of the packing
houses in the first operating season, two cold stores would be established
by mid-1975. To meet this deadline, planning and engineering works would
start immediately after effectiveness. HPMC would contract suitable engineer-
ing consultants (most probably an Indian firm) immediately upon its establish-
ment and this would be a condition of effectiveness. Appendix 1 may serve
as a basis for terms of reference for these consultants. The first two cold
storage units would be attached to the existing AIC cold store in Delhi. A
third unit would be added to this cold storage complex within the following
year so that it would be operating in the 1976 season. Another cold store
would be built simultaneously in Calcutta. The basic design of these cold
stores, i.e. one-story vs. multi-story construction, would be determined
by analyzing the cost structure, particularly the trade-offs between site
costs, construction and equipment costs, and operating costs. A plan of a
multi-story apple cold store, as recently built in Bombay, is shown on Chart
WB-7266. Equipment suppliers would provide a resident engineer to instruct
and supervise during installation and the first three months of operation.
5.        Procedures for Processing Plant.  As can be learned from paragraphs
22 and 23 of Annex 5, the juice concentrate plant would require fairly
sophisticated equipment. Since only one Indian firm, Vulcan Laval Ltd.,
in Poona produces part of this kind of equipment, procurement would be made
through international competitive bidding. As this would be the first
modern apple juice concentrate plant in India, the engineering know-how
would have to be imported. To have the plant operate as early as possible,
planning and engineering works would be started immediately after effective-
ness. A condition of effectiveness would be that HPMC would have a suitable
engineering team under contract. This team would work closely together
with two of the foreign consultants who would be assigned to HPMC under the
technical assistance program (Annex 7 and WB-7321(3R)), the Senior Food
Technologist and the Food Micro-biologist, and these two specialists should
preferably be employees of the same consultant firm. In this way engineer-
ing and training would be integrated from the very beginning of the planning
stage. The engineering consultants should also take into account the
findings of a survey to be made on the product specifications desired by
potential distributors of the apple juice. Detailed terms of reference
for the engineering consultants are given in Appendix 2.

to HPMC;

and

2.        Preparation of Tender Documents.  Tender documents would be
prepared under the direction of HPMC. In the case of the cold stores and
the juice plant, the engineering consultants would be responsible for
preparing all the documents for tendering of the construction and equip-
ment supply and installation contracts. The tender documents for both
civil works and equipment would include, inter alia:
(a) Bidding advertisement and tender invitations;
(b) General contract conditions;
(c) Special contract conditions;
(d) Technical specifications;

(i) Form of construction contract; and
(j) Questionnaires and qualification forms as needed for
qualification of contractors.
The above documents shall show clearly all requirements necessary to carry
out the construction contracts and shall conform to accented Indian
engineering practices. For the cold stores, the engineering consultants
shall submit all documents to HPMC for consultation, review and approval
before having tender documents provided in final form. The engineering
consultants would have the major responsibility of providing clarification
of the tender documents to inquiring construction contractors and equipment
suppliers, and notification of such clarifications to all other contractors
and suppliers.
3.        Site Investigations.  The engineers concerned shall examine and
review all maps, charts and geological information available from the
government and the municipality. They shall then check the accuracy of these
data to provide basic information for preliminary project assessment.  They
shall take soil samples and have conducted soil tests in a reliable institute
or laboratory, as required by the materials obtained. If several alternative
sites are available, consultants shall make recommendations on final site
selection only after the engineering design and the financial analysis have
been completed.
Specific Additional Terms of Reference for Cold Storage Consultants
4.        Preliminary Engineering Design.  Considering the site(s) available,
the consultants shall prepare and evaluate alternative layouts. They shall
take into account operational requirements to determine:
(a) The construction materials and techniques best suited for
the site giving particular consideration to the outside
temperatures;
(b) The type of refrigeration equipment taking into account its
anticipated load and the availability of service and spare
parts;
(c) The type of stand-by generator;
(d) The availability of utilities.
The drawings in the consultants' report shall give the dimens:ons aad
typical cross-sections of the building and vaJor features of the proposed
design. The consultants shall also propose specif ications for suitable
equipment taking into account spare parts availability and service situation.
5.        Time Schedule and Reports.  The consultants shall submit to HPIMC:



ANNEX 8
Appendix 1
Page 4
(a)  A Draft Report - within three months after the starting date,
summarizing all works performed, findings and recommendations
for site, layout, design and implementation.
(b) A Final Report - within one month following receipt of the
HPMC's comments on the final draft report, incorporating
all tne necessary revisions and accompanied by all the
drawings and desiga data.
In addition to the above, the consuetants shall, at any time, furnish
E.PMC ulth such information as it may reasonably request to follow the
progress of the work. All these reports and documents shall become the
exclusive property of HPMC.
6.        Preparation of Tender Documents Bid Evaluation.  The consultants
shall be responsible for preparing all the documents required for
tendering the construction and equipment contracts. They shall submit to
WPMC draft tender documents (which shall cover all items listed in paragraph
2) one month after the final report. Within 15 days following receipt of
HPMC comments on the draft documents the consultants shall present the
final documents which shall incorporate the necessary revisions. They shall
also help evaluate bids.
7.        Execution.  The consultants shall provide an engineer to
supervise construction and installation of equipment, test runs and
adjustment of equipment. This engineer shall make the final inspection
and submit the acceptance documents to HPMC.

HIMACHAL PRADESH APPLE PROCESSING AND MARKETING PROJECT
Detailed Terms of Reference for Juice Concentrate
Plant Engineering Consultants
General Scope of Work
1.        An engineering consultant firm would be contracted before
effectiveness of the Credit to assist HPMC in planning and establishing
the proposed juice concentrate plant. The general duties of the consultants
would be as follows:
(a)  Determine the functional requirements of the plant taking
fully into account the results of a market survey to be
conducted (Main Report, para 3.10);
(b)  Recommend the optimal general location of the plant for
approval by HPMC;
(c)  Obtain field information for alternative sites available:
(1) topography and soil,
(2) availability and location of utilities, particularly
water and electricity,
(3) road and rail connections;
(d)  Prepare preliminary layouts for approval by HPMC;
(e)  Prepare preliminary cost estimates for construction, equipment
and maintenance;
(f)  Make financial analysis of future plant operations, including
a financial forecast (income statement and cash flow
projections) for the first five years of operation (by months
for the operating seasons to show the working capital
requirements);
(g)  If and when results of previous work has been approved
by HPMC, prepare detailed design drawings;
(h)  Prepare specificati6ns and layouts;
(i)  Prepare tender documents, including forms for contractors'
qualifications in consultation with IDA through PWD;



ANNEX 8
Appendix 2
Page 2
(j) Prepare tender documents, including forms for contractors'
qualifications in consultation with IDA through PWD;
(k) Receive, analyze and tabulate tenders which would be
made to HPMC;
(1) Recommend specific site and layout based upon proposed
capacity and product mix as well as storage and process
design and flow diagram for approval by HPMC;
(m) Recommend awards to HPMC;
(n) Revise financial forecast;
(o) Assign at least one engineer to supervise the work and
performance of all civil, mechanical and electrical
contractors;
(p)  Review and follow through contractors' and suppliers'
schedules;
(q) Check computations, prepare change orders, examine and
certify contractors' applications for payments;
(r) Advise and assist HPMC and contractors;
(s) Upon completion of the works, furnish record drawings
of the project "as built and installed" and assemble cata-
logues, operation manuals, and written guarantees to be
supplied by the contractors;
(t)  Supervise and review the test runs and adjustment of
machinery; and
(u) Make final inspection and submit acceptance documents.
2.        The consultants shall cooperate fully with HPMC which will provide
the necessary data, facilities and services. The consultants shall, however,
be solely responsible for the analysis and interpretation of all data received
and for the findings, conclusions and recommendations contained in their
reports. The consultants shall first carry out a preliminary study to
recommend a specific site, layout, process design and flow diagram (k) above,
and will continue when HPMC approves those recommendations.
Preliminary Study
3.        Site Investigations.  Consultants shall start with a location analysis
taking into account functional requirements of the plant as laid down by
the Senior Marketing Expert (working under separate contract) and approved by



ANNEX 8
Appendix 2
Page 3
HPMC and the best availability of raw material. After having identified
the general plant location and received the approval of HPMC, the consultants
shall analyze several sites as to their specific suitability. They shall
examine and review all maps, aerial photographs, charts, all weather, geologi-
cal and transportation information available from the government through
HPMC. The consultants shall then conduct site investigations which shall
include, inter alia:
(a) Topographical Survey to check the accuracy of existing
topographical maps and charts and to provide basic
information for preliminary site assessment;
(b) Soil Survey including, if needed, soil tests;
(c) Climate Survey to obtain monthly records of minimum and
maximum temperatures, precipitation, humidity and wind;
(d) Utilities Survey. The avaiLlability, location and cost
structure of utilities has to be examined for each alternative
site;
(e) Transportation Survey to obtain information on road and rail
connections from the supply and to the consumer area; and
(f) Sewage Survey.
The consultants may entrust some of the field survey and laboratory
studies to subcontractors or institutions subject to HPMC's approval. The
consultants, however, shall remain solely responsible for the supervision,
adequacy and accuracy of the work, for the analytical interpretation of all
data received and for the conclusions and recommendations.
4.        Alternative Plans.  Based on the findings of the site selection
procedures, the consultants shall outline preliminary plans for the alterna-
tive sites available. These plans shall include a process design, an
engineering flow diagram, preliminary layouts for each alternative site,
cost estimates for construction, equipment and maintenance as well as a
financial analysis of future plant operations.
5.        The consultants shall prepare a report which shall contain their
recommendations on the specific site, process design, flow diagram and
layout proposed.
6.        This report shall be submitted within three months of the starting
date.
7.        HPMC will review the consultants' report within a month after
receipt. In consultation with the Association HPMC will either confirm
or modify the proposal.



ANNEX 8
Appendix 2
Page 4
Pant Design
8.        As soon as HPMC approves a preliminary proposal for the plant,
the consultants shall prepare a plant design. In doing this the consultants
shall determine the most economical type of construction and operation. The
desigr. drawings shall give the dimensions and typical cross-sections of all
structures and major featureb of the design, in sufficient detail to provide
an adequate basis for preparing tender documents. Preparation shall include
inter alia:
(-) Detailed design drawings;
(b)  Specifications for all structures;
(c) Specifications of all equipment which rnay be necessary for
an economicaL juice processing plant;
(d)  Detailed instructions and recommendations for reliable
equipment installation;
(e) Recommendations with regard to the kind and quantity of
manpower, materials and tools which the equipment supplier
will have to provide;
(f) Detailed technology descriptions, including process designs
and quality indicators for materials to be used, technological
instructions for the staff explaining in detail and understand-
able form the functioning of the plant to ensure an efficient
operation of the plant;
(g) Maintenance and repair instructions including "clean-in-place"
(CIP) instructions;
(h) Construction plans including equipment layout and piping
diagrams;
(i) Utilities diagrams and plans;
(j) Flow diagrams for fruit juices; service, cleaning and
drinking water; steam; coolants;
(k) Plans for tubing.
(1) Proposal for waste disposal;
(m)  Preventive maintenance program.
9.        The consultants st.all analyse prospective costs and revenues of
the plant assuming good operating procedures.  The consultants shall base



ANNEX 8
Appendix 2
Page 5
the financial analysis on estimates of annual capital and recurrent costs
and conduct breakeven calculations taking into account, inter alia, raw
material prices, capacity utilization and sales prices.
10.       The consultants shall prepare a project preparation report which
shall contain all the information outlined above. This report shall be
submitted within two months after HPMC has decided to establish the plant
and informed the consultants accordingly.
Preparation of Tender Documents
11.       Im,nediately after project preparation the consultants shall
prepare all the documents for tendering the construction and equipment supply
and installation contracts, including inter alia:
(a) Bidding advertisement and tender invitations;
(b) General contract conditions;
(c) Special contract conditions;
(d) Technical specifications including arrangements with local
contractors for civil works;
(e) Design drawings required for bidding and construction:
(i) Structural engineering designs;
(ii) Civil engineering layouts including site drawings; and
(iii) Machinery layout and specifications;
(f) InstructiLon to bidders;
(g) Form of contractor's bids (bid form);
(h) Form of construction contract; and
(i) Questionnaires and qualification forms as needed for
qualification of contractors.
The above documents shall show clearly all requirements necessary to carry
out the construction contracts and shall conform to accepted Indian
engineering practices. The consultants shall submit all documents to HPMC
for consultation, review and approval before having tender documents
provided in final form. The engineering consultants would have the major
responsibility of providing clarification of the tender documents to inquiring
suppliers, and notification of such clarifications to all other contractors
and suppliers.



ANNEX 8
Appendix 2
Page 6
Bid Evaluation
12.       The consultants shall assist HPMC in analyzing the bids and make
recommendations as to award of contract. The consultants (as well as HPMC)
shall base this procedure on the most recent version of the "Guidelines for
Procurement under World Bank Loans and IDA Credits", Sections 4.2 to 4.10.
Execution
13.       Supervision.  The engineering consultants shall provide a resident
engineer at the location of the future plant to supervise construction and
equipment installation. The responsibilities of the resident engineer are
spelled out in para 1, m-s.
14.       Initial Operation.  Both the engineering consultants and the
equipment supplier shall provide one resident engineer each at the plant
location during the first operating season of about three months to supervise
test runs and adjustment of machinery and to assist in training the plant
staff. The engineering consultant shall, towards the end of the first
operating season, make the final inspection and submit the acceptance documents
to HPMC.
Time Estimates

or several engineers, for some periods on a full-time basis and for other
