ANNEX 1 1
Page 1
INDIA
HIMACHAL PRADESH APIPLE PROCESSING AND NARKETING PROJECT
Horticultural Produce Processing and Marketing
Corporation
A. Background
General Objectives
1.        The major objectives of the project are to improve the marketing
of apples in India, and in HP in particular; to bring about higher incomes
to growers; and to deliver to consumers fruits of higher quality at current
or lower prices. The task is demanding, because production is projected to
grow at about 14% per year during 1972-78, a rate consideraly higher than
the 9% consumption increase estimated on the basis of India's annual urban
population growth rate of about 5%, annual average per capita income growth
of 2.5%, and an income elasticity of 1.5 for demand for apples.
2.        While the rate of production increase is expected to decelerate
from 24.5% in 1972 to 9.0% after 1978, and there will probably be no pres-
sure for the demand gap to increase, farmgate apple prices are likely to
suffer severely unless systematic efforts are made to tide over the anticipated
demand shortfall in the next five years or so. This medium-run demand short-
fall can be at least partially offset by measures designed to: (i) extend
the marketing period; and (ii) expand the market into cities hitherto largely
untapped.
Project Program
3.        Without carrying out a coordinated program designed to enhance
marketing efficiency, it would not be possible in future to keep the grower's
income per unit of output from seriously declining, in view of the large
increases in supplies coming forward over the next few years. The project
program is designed: (i) to extend the marketing period of the fruit through
earlier deliveries to the terminal markets, using a faster forwardin.g service
and withdrawing some supplies from the fresh apple market and cold storing
them for off-season marketing; (ii) to expand the market into cities hitherto
largely untapped; (iii) to reduce marketing and transportation costs, both
explicit and implicit, in particular the intermediary margins; (iv) to
improve apple quality and its public image by introducing quality control
and adopting in due course higher grading standards; (v) to utilize the
large quantities of culls now being wasted, for commercial production of
apple juice concentrate; (vi) perhaps the most important of all, to establish
a marketing organization engaged in trading apples and apple products that



ANNEX 1 1
Page 2
would procure them through straight purchase and consignment, and sell them
through its own wholesale organization, thereby offering the growers an
alternative to the existing unsatisfactory wholesale channels with the com-
mission agents, and their current malpractices.
B. Operations
General
4.        The project calls for a number of interrelated and coordinated
activities: (i) procurement; (ii) grading and packing; (iii) cold storage
units; (iv) manufacture of apple juice concentrate; (v) forwarding;
(vi) trading of off-season cold stored apples; (vii) collective marketing
of fresh apples; (viii) credit to farmers; and (ix) credit to traders.
Procurement
5.        Procurement of apples would best be done at the packing houses.
HPMC would canvass growers in the area as well as the "pre-harvest con-
tractors", offer firm prices for specific variety grades as early as possible,
and enter into firm contracts. During the early operational phase, HPMC
should enlist support from SDH and the Fruit Development Board to encourage
growers to participate.
Grading and Packing
6.        The packing operation is a pre-condition for all of the activities
indicated. The primary function of packing houses would be to introduce
appropriate quality control for apple marketing. It would standardize the
grading, packing, and packing materials, facilitate coordinated forwarding
of the fruit, provide a ready medium to embody innovations in more efficient
and economical packing materials, and serve as the channel for the culls
to be sold and transported to the juice concentrate plant.
7.        The total capacity of the packing houses, despite a practically
unlimited economy of scale on the packing operation itself, is limited by
the volume of apple production within technically determined radiuses in
the areas of concentrated production (Annex 5). The combined capacity
appropriate to the time horizon for which production can be projected with
reasonable certainty is an input of about 55,000 tons per season for the
twelve packing houses. This would be about 40% of the output of the project
districts, or about 22% of estimated total production in HP for 1978. The
net output of the project's packing houses would be 40,000 tons per season.
This is large enough to enter into the market with sufficient impact.
The capacity of each house ranges between 27 and 46% of the total output of
the district it covers, its throughput requirements would not be difficult
to procure.



ANNNEX  1
Page .3
8.        The packing houses, however, require heavy investment in plants
and equipment. Packing quality would be substantially improved, but the
unit packing cost is likely to be somewhat higher than that of the grower,
since he packs without (in most instances) hiring any paid labor or making
any cash outlay on facilities. Although a packing house would have a
captive market of about 50% of its capacity, it is highly desirable for it
to operate at or near its full capacity, if the project objectives are to
be attained. Since the project as a whole is viable, it should be possible
for HPMC to provide a xackin- service to outside customers starting at
rates substantially lower than its full cost (in order to make the service
attractive to growers) for an initial promotional period of, say, three to
four years, during which rates would be raised to cover the full cost.
9.        The full cosz of packing 40,000 tons is about Rs 15 million, or
about Rs 6.92 per 18 kg case. Of this, Rs 5.17 is for the packing material.
While HPMC would charge the full cost to its own packing, it could probably
attract enough apples for full capacity operation if it charged to the
growers, say, Rs 4.00 for the first year, Rs 5.00 for the second year,
Rs 6.00 for the third year, and up to the full cost of Rs 7.00 beginning
the fourth year. Since the bulk of the throughput during the promotional
period would be for HPMC's own apples to which the full rate would be
applied, the operating losses from concessionary rates would not be exces-
sively large. At the anticipated concessionary rates, as shown in Table 1,
the packing operation is expected to accumulate losses for the first three
years, and then to start generating moderate annual net profits of about
Rs 400,000, and emerge with a positive cumulative profit in the eleventh
year. The financial rate of return on this component is 14%.
10.       Quality control would be implemented in the packing houses by SDH
much as provided for in the Agriculture Produce Marketing Act of 1937.
Official horticultural inspectors would be assigned to the houses during
the season to enforce the designated quality standards for fruit and packing
materials.
Cold Storage Units
11.       The cold storage component of the project is intended to:  (a) make
it possible to withdraw a sizeable portion of the apple supply during the
fresh apple marketing period, to relieve the downward pressure on fresh
apple prices likely to arise as a result of the projected demand shortfall;
and (b) to profit from the attractive off-season prices, and use the ad-
ditional income to cover a substantial portion of HPMC's overheads.
12.       The design of each cold storage unit calls for a number of important
considerations. Its volume must be large enough to have the intended impact
on fresh apple prices, and to exploit the economies of scale in cold storage
costs. From the commercial point of view, however, it should not be so large
as to spoil the off-season prices, or to make it difficult for HPMC to
purchase the required quantity of fruit at tenable prices, for the attempt
at nrempting a significant portion of project area output may give rise to
an inordinate price increase.



ANNEX 1 1
Page 4
13.       Pla-n.ing the cold itorage capacity on the assumption that a service
would be provided on a rental basis would not be practical because of:
(a) uncertaintv over E e degree of utilization for storing apples; (b) the
need to maintain the identities of nmnerous individual lots both for owner-
ship and quality; (c) the probable complications in marketing strategy;
and (d) the scope for disputes arising from liability for quality deteriora-
tion and apportionment of sales proceeds, etc. The bulk of the cold storage
apples would thus have co be owned by HPMC. Bdecause of these considerations,
the project's total cold storage capacity estimated to be commercially safe
is about 8,000 tons td-._W-, .ogether with the 2,000 torn unit in Delhi and
anorne: of 2,000 tons i-  Bonbay already built by the Agro-Industries
Corpora-on, would bring the total capacity to 12,000 tons.
14.       Since existing Indian cold storage installations are not fully
suitable for apple storage, new units with adequate temperature and humidity
control would have to be installed. It is extremely important for HPMC's
off-season marketing activities that the apples offered for sale were of
the highest quality on the market. The commercial success of the project
depends very heavily on HPMC's ability to maintain this quality for its
off-season apples.
15.       The advantages and disadvantages of having the cold storage units
in the producing areas or at terminal markets were considered. It was
concluded that they should be located in Delhi and Calcutta. The overriding
consideration was their off-season use. In the producing areas they could
only be used for apples, because nothing else requiring cold storage is
produced there in sufficient quantities. At terminal markets they could be
used for storing potatoes and other commodities when not used for apples.
The bulk of the new capacity, 6,000 out of 8,000 tons, would be located in
Delhi to maximize both the multi-purpose usefulness and the flexibility
of choice between the various terminal markets for the final dispatch.
Since AIC already has a 2,000 ton unit in Bombay, the remaining 2,000 ton
unit would be in Calcutta. An additional important consideration in locating
most of the capacity in Delhi has been that the harvested fruits can be
delivered there without being excessively heated en route. During off-
season marketing the temperature en route becomes substantially cooler, so
that overheating is avoided.
16.       Income projections for the cold storage units are given in Table
2. The fairly high rate of return of this activity of 27% is based on
conservative assumptions.
Apple Juice Concentrate
17.       At oresent, large quantities of culls, which make up about 20% of
total productiDn, go to waste. Commercial utilization of them has been very
earnestly soughrt by the apile industry and the HP Government.  Under the
project's packing house systen, culls would be a ready source of raw
material for a concertrate .:ant.  The market potential for apple juice
concentrate appears to be quite good, and the bottling plants which have



ANNEX 11
Page 5
proliferated in India in recent years with Cocoa Cola and other ready-to-
serve beverages seem to be a logical market outlet for the product. An
arrangement for sole distributorship with an established marketing firm
would be the best solution for mzrketing the product. However, HPMC should
be prepared later to sell to individual bottling plants, or even to bottle
itself. Income projections for the juice concentrate plant are given in
Table 3.
Forwarding
18.       Forwarding activities would be one of the key components of the
project. There are three important reasons for this. First, as shown in
Table 4, a significant reduction in the cost of transportation is feasible,
and any possible reduction under a large scale marketing operation would
be an important incentive to growers to use HPMC for collective marketing
and, therefore, for packing their fruits at HPMC's packing houses. Second,
the shortening of the shipping time for the fruits to reach the terminal
markets would extend the marketing period by making it possible to get
the fruits on the market about two weeks earlier. This, together with the
off-season marketing out of cold storage, would play a critical role in
filling the anticipated demand gap. Third, the operation would provide
the badly needed protection against weather and rough handling, to which
the fruits are extremely vulnerable. A further important benefit from the
operation would be the intelligence gained on fruit movements to the various
destinations. Income projections for the forwarding operation are given
in Table 5.
Trading of Cold Stored Apples
19.       Since it is imperative to extend the marketing period by with-
drawing fruits into cold storage for marketing them off-season (an activity
that calls for HPMC to possess and own the fruit), HPMC would buy fruit for
storage purposes. Fortunately, the off-season prices have been quite
attractive and, since the quality of the cold stored apples would be superior
to any competitors', the operation is expected to be quite profitable, even
on very conservative assumptions for buying and selling prices and various
marketing costs. This would be an entirely new channel through which growers
could sell their fruit at definite prices and for cash. The assumptions and
the income projections for this operation are given in Tables 6 and 7.
Collective Marketing of Fresh Apples
20.       Since off-season marketing requires its own organization, and HPMC
would already be engaged in forwarding operations, its sales offices would
provide marketing services to growers interested in selling their fruits
through a channel other than the conventional consignment. This would be
done on a commission basis, and the financial projections and assumptions
are given in Tables 8 and 9.
21.       With the two types of trading operations outlined above, growers
would have a choice of options: (i) to market their apples through con-
ventional channels controlled by the commission agents; (ii) to sell their



ANNEX 11
Page 6
fruits to h-PMC for cash to meet its cola storage requirements; and (iii) to
enzrust their fruits to h?MC for collective marketing on concessionary
commission rates and with advantageous advance payment arrangements.  As shown
in the chart appended to Annex 4, all apples marketed under the project would
by-pass the conmission agents (as distinguished from the wholesalers), and
would be sold for definite prices, while the sales would be directly between
HPMC's sales offices and the wholesalers and subwholesalers, avoiding the
exorbitant and repetit.ve commission charges and the concomittant malpractices.
22.       The competitiveness of HPMC's marketing would be greater in terminal
markets other than Delhi, because the scope for a reduction in transportation
cost and the elimination of intermediary margins would be greater the more
distant the market. HPMC should not, however, overlook the importance of
the Delhi market, because without a strong base there it would neither be
able to maintain the necessary operational flexibility for ultimate distri-
bution of its fruits nor to acquire the critical market intelligence.
Credit to Farmers
23.       Under the existing system of consignment sale, the bulk of the
growers obtain advances free of interest from commission agents at the time
of fruit setting (Annex 4, para 28). The amount is usually up to Rs 6.00
per case for the estimated production. Without providing similar credit,
HPMC is unlikely to acquire sufficient fruits. It should, therefore,
advance at least Rs 10.00 per case in, say, four monthly installments
immediately following the fruit setting, and obtain reimbursement at the
time of sale. it would, of course, pay the full amount upon taking possession
of fruit purc>ased on its own account for off-season marketing. In addition,
the packing and forwarding services would have to be provided on credit,
and payment made on realization of the proceeds. These credit facilities
would be the main incentive to farmers to market their fruits through HPMC
during its early period; at a later stage other benefits could be considered.
Credit to Traders
24.       HPMC would build up its own distribution network for marketing
both fresh and cold storage apples. Its main distributors should be the
existing Mashakhoras and small wholesalers. Since they now procure their
fruits on credit from the commission agents, HPMC would offer at least
as good credit facilities. A one-month term for such credit should be
adequate.
C. Organization
25.      HIPMC's entire operation would require close coordination of its
cui,e diverse activities. The buying, selling and contracting of goods
and services would require procedures under wlhich authority is sufficiently



ANNEX 1 1
Page 7
delegated to units operating at different levels. HPMC should be both auto-
nomous and integrated. The management would have full power to appoint and
remove its employees, set up its own accounting procedures, and establish
its own wage and salary scales and other employee benefits, etc. At nego-
tiations IDA and the Indian delegation agreed on the Articles of Association
of HMPC.
26.       HPMC would be a fully-owned subsidiary of AIC and an autonomous,
commercially-oriented enterprise. The board would be composed of 15
directors, of which 8 would be appointed by AIC; 5 by HP government,
including the chairman and representatives of the Departments of Finance and
Agriculture, PWD and SDH; and one by the HP Fruit Development Board; the
managing director would also be a member of the board.
27.       Proposed organization of the staff and employees is reflected in
Table 10. Project success depends on competent and dedicated management
and employees. It has been assumed that by offering competitive salaries
and responsible positions, HPMC would be able to recruit the competent and
dedicated personnel necessary for commercial operation.
D. Finance
28.       Annual cost projections for the Read and Branch Offices are given
in Table 11. Consolidated income statement projections covering the various
activities are the basis upon which the financial rate of return has been
calculated (Annex 13).
E. HPMC's Impact on Oversupply
29.       On the basis of projected production and estimated growth rate of
demand of about 9% a year, the estimated oversupply of apples without the
project during the traditional marketing season would be:
Year                Thousand tons
1973                      10
1974                      18
1975                       27
1976                      32
1977                       35
1978                      39
Project implementation would mean that about 5,000 tons would be marketed
ahead of and at least 10,000 tons after the conventional marketing period,
so that the oversupply would be reduced considerably. Some of the remaining
oversupply would be reduced by sales in cities virtually untapped previously
as apple markets.

ANNEX 12
Page 1
INTDIA
HDMACHAL PRADESH APPLE PROCESSING AND MARKETING PROJECT
Project Evaluation Procedures
1.        This would be the first Bank/IDA project setting up a large-scale
integrated system aimed at improving processing and marketing of a perishable
horticultural crop. It is tnerefore very important to IDA as well as to the
project that evaluation procedures are provided for and funded.
2.        Assurances were obtained from HP Government that it would enter
into a contract with an appropriate institution, such as the University of
Simla, the National Council of Applied Economic Research or the Directorate
of MIarketing and Inspection, which would evaluate the impact of the project.
The contract would specify: (a) the senior research officer who would be
in charge of the evaluation as well as the personnel who would collect the
data and perform the analysis; (b) the time period for data collection,
for analysis and for reporting; and (c) the compensation to be paid to the
institute by the Government and the timing of such payments.
3.        Evaluation should commence immediately after the effective-
ness of the credit, so that data can be collected on the operations of the
present system and compared with those of the new system after full develop-
ment of the market facilities. Suggested terms of reference for the
evaluation appear as Appendix 1, and costs in Annex 9, Table 7.






ANNEX 12
Appendix 1
Page 1
INDIA
HIMACHAL PRADESH APPLE PROCESSING AND MARKETING PROJECT
Tentative Terms of Reference for an
Evaluation of the Project's Impact
Purpose
1.        To examine the changing marketing pattern and to estimate the
benefits resulting from the project.
Scope
2.        The behavior of the following factors would be specifically
evaluated before and after introduction of the new system.
(a) detailed production data, particularly area under apples
(irrigated and rainfed) and apple yields, by varieties;
(b) farm-gate prices;
(c) losses incurred in transporting the apples from farms to
the terminal markets;
(d) handling losses at all stages between harvesting and
consumption of the apples with particular emphasis on
culls and their disposal;
(e) storage losses;
(f) processing and marketing costs, including storage costs;
(g) quantities of apples marketed by project area patwars,
varieties and years and breakdown of apple throughputs
of the various project facilities;
(h) weekly pattern of marketings;
(i) market intelligence available to growers on prices, supplies,
production, etc.;
(j)  market intelligence available to traders (wholesalers, sub-
wholesalers and retailers) on prices, supplies, etc.;
(k) quantities and pattern of dispatches of apples to terminal
markets;



ANUEX 12
Appendix 1
Page 2
(1) all trade margins between farm-gate and consumer prices, by
varieties, patwars, consumer markets, and weeks;
(m) number of market functionaries (i.e. wholesalers, subwhole-
salers, retailers, and all employees of those, etc.);
(n)  quantities of apples channelled through the project
facilities - as proportion of total quantities marketed;
(o) growers' motivations as to (1) using alternative market
channels, and (2) determining their production programs
with particular emphasis on apple cultivation;
(p) the size of growers using HPMCIs facilities; and
(q) consumer behavior and competing products.
In addition, the evaluation would focus on the impact of HPMC on the current
ralpractices in the distribution channel.
Research Methodology
3.        Five catchment areas of packing houses constructed in the first
two years of the credit disbursement would be selected for evaluation. The
selected areas would be appropriate - two in Simla District, two in Kulu
District and one in Miandi District - to allow maximum variability in the
volume and area of their operations. Data collection would commence well
before the first apple harvest season after the date of effectiveness, i.e.,
prior to the construction of the facilities and would continue for two years
subsequent to the end of the disbursement period. This would be essential
since the purpose of the study would be to compare the performance of the
existing and the project systems, and since changes in the marketing patterns
will occur only gradually over time.
4.        One field investigator would be assigned per area studied.  If
he cannot be located in the area, he should be able to make frequent
trips to the market yard, e.g., one trip per month in the off-season and
a continuous stay in the area from June to November.
5.        An additional investigator would be assigned to study all activi-
ties connected with the transshipment centers in Parwanu and at the Delhi/
Iharyana border, and another three investigators would be working in the three
dominant terminal markets: Delhi, Calcutta and Bombay. These investigators
would start their studies at the same time as the investigators in the
packing house areas. While the investigator assigned to the transshipment
centers would stay in the field from June to late October continually, and
during the rest of the year would be there twice a month, the investigators
in the terminal markets woa.Ld conduct field work all year round.



ANNEX 12
Appendix 1
Page 3
6.        The data gathering would be a combination of statistical information
and direct observation.
7.        In gathering information the following requirements should receive
prime attention. Every effort should be made to collect, at least weekly,
prices series and marketing costs for major apple varieties originating
in Himachal Pradesh, Uttar Pradesh axd Jamu and Kashmir, and also for other
domestic fruits. The survey should cover all processing and marketing
activities between harvesting and consumption as completely as possible.
8.        If it is not feasible to collect data from all the traders on their
purchase and sale operations, data from at least five major wholesalers and
subwholesalers each, and a multiple of retailers should be collected.
9.        Direct observation would involve maintenance of a diary by each
investigator with detailed information on the following factors:
(A) In the packing house areas:
(a) names and apple areas (by varieties) of each grower;
(b) major marketing arrangements for each grower (preharvest
contract, commission agents, alternative project
channels, etc.);
(c) terminal markets to which supplies of each grower are
shipped and, if possible, name of wholesaler;
(d) population of the area;
(e) description of the road network connecting the
producer areas with the major highway system;
(f) complete throughput records for all forwarding
agents in the area;
(g) complete throughput records for the packing house;
(h) as many price records as possible;
(i) transport arrangements, particularly truck load
records;
(;) interviews of growers regarding their views on project
system vs. conventional system;
(k) description of availability of market intelligence
to growers.



ANNEX 12
Appendix 1
Page 4
(B) In the transshipment centers:
(a) number of incoming and outgoing truck loads by area
of origin and destination, varieties, and, as often
as possible, by forwarding agent, grower and wholesaler;
(b) duration of stay of each lot and storage losses in
transshipment center;
(c) utilization of truck capacities and arrangements of
truckers to increase utilization by transporting other
commodities;
(d) records on uses of transshipment center during the
off-season.
(C) In the terminal markets:
(a) throughput of a representative sample of wholesalers,
subwholesalers and retailers, at least by weeks during
the Uttar Pradesh, Himachal Pradesh and Jammu and
Kashmir harvest seasons and semi-monthly during the
off-season, and by varieties;
(b) purchasing and selling prices related to the above
throughputs, by States (and for Himachal Pradesh, as
frequently as possible, by areas) of origin;
(c)  cost structure pertinent to apple marketing of the
above sample of traders;
(d) description of buying, selling and storage arrangements
of these traders showing the proportions of major market
channels;
(e) interviews with traders regarding their views on the
project and their inclinations to cooperate or compete
with the project;
(f) interviews with a stratified sample of consumers
regarding their preference for certain apple varieties,
processed apple products and other major fruits, prices
paid for these goods, willingness to buy cold storage
apples in the off-season and at which prices (to prepare
demand and price projections), etc.
Analysis of the Data
10.       The data collected would be analyzed by the senior research
officer with the support of the investigators and, if possible, other



ANNIEX 12
Appendix 1
Page 5
research workers attached to the institute. A report containing the principal
data and the findings of the analysis would be made available to HP Govern-
ment annually as a basis for reviewing the marketing strategy, and to IDA as
a basis for preparing similar projects.

ANNEX 14
Page 1
INDIA
HIMACHAL PRADESH APPLE PROCESSING AND MARKETING PROJECT
Economic Evaluation
A. Introduction
1.        At full development the project would provide an integrated
and efficient processing and marketing system for about 55,000 tons of
apples annually (40,000 tons net of culls), or about one-fourth of total
HP production in 1977. Of these 40,000 net tons, 20,000 tons would be
customer apples which HPMC would grade and pack for a fee; 10,000 tons
would be purchased by HPMC for cold storage; and 10,000 tons would be
fresh-traded apples graded, packed, transported and marketed by HPMC on
consignment (i.e. the growers receive HPMC's sales price less fixed
deductions).  In addition, 11,000 tons of otherwise-wasted culls would be
processed into juice concentrate.
2.        Although the cold storage and cull apples would actually be
purchased by HPMC, whereas the customer and consignment apples would not
be, in order to make sensitivity testing easier and to make clearer the
relative magnitude of different costs and benefits, all apples are treated
as if HPMC did not buy them, but only handled them on consignment.
B. Costs
General
3.        Economic costs of the project, subject to the adjustments
indicated, would include: (a) all investment and replacement costs (for
processing and marketing facilities; road maintenance equipment, cableways,
road works, technical assistance, training and the project evaluation study);
(b) "permanent" working capital requirements of HPMC (for stocks) of
Rs 20 million, which would be acquired as capital subscriptions over the
first four years; and (c) operating costs of HPMC and road maintenance
costs of PWD.
Adjustments
4.        Import duties, taxes and price contingencies are, of course,
excluded, as are intra-HPMC transfer payments. Thus, of HPMC's full
development annual operating cost of Rs 69.6 million (see Annex 13, Table 1),
Rs 20.3 million paid to farmers and Rs 18.6 million of internal transfers



ANNEX 14
Page 2
are subtracted to get Rs 30.7 million of economic costs. The payment to
farmers consists of Rs 2.8 million by the Juice Concentrate Plant, Rs 12.0
million by the Cold Storage Apple Trading and Rs 5.5 million by the Fresh
Apple Trading section of HPMC. The transfer payments would be as follows
(see Annex 11, Tables 1, 2, 3, 5, 7, and 9):
From                           To                              Amount
(Rs million)
Cold Storage Apple Trading          Packing Stations                   3.85
Cold Storage Apple Trading          Forwarding Operations              4.14
Cold Storage Apple Trading          Cold Store                         2.64
Fresh Apple Trading                 Packing Stations                   3e85
Fresh Apple Trading                 Forward Operations                 3.58
Forwarding Operations               Packing Stations                   0.56
Total       18.62
5.        Based on India's indirect tax system, the opportunity cost of
$1.00 of foreign exchange is estimated to be about Rs 10, so foreign
exchange costs are converted into Rupee costs at that rate (see para 6.05).
Labor does not appear to be in excess supply at existing wages, so no
adjustment is made in the wage rates.
C. Benefits
6.        The project's benefits would result from the following:
(a) more efficient grading, packing and marketing of apples;
(b) more even temporal and regional spread of the apple
marketing;
(c) improved transportation links to the apple-growing
areas; and
(d) utilization of the otherwise-wasted cull apple for
juice concentrate.
Wholesale Premiums
7.        The 20,000 tons (net of culls) of apples that are graded and
packed by HPMC for a fee would benefit from more consistent grading (which
saves wholesalers from having to regrade) and sturdier packing (which
reduces damage) and should command at least a 5% wholesale premium over
conventionally packed and graded apples.



ANNEX 14
Page 3
8.        The cold-storage apples would command a premium of about 37%,
primarily because they would be marketed during the off season. All of
this price increase would represent an economic benefit because consumers
value apples more when they are scarce. (However, any price increases for
apples still sold in the main peak-season markets which result from the
overall supply reduction are primarily an income transfer from consumers to
producers, and hence are not an economic benefit.) The consignment apples
should sell for at least a 13% wholesale premium over conventionally-
marketed ones (based on a 15% retail premium, but larger dealer margins),
as about half of them would get to market somewhat before the peak season
as a result of faster distribution. Furthermore, these will be the
choicest of the apples, and the faster distribution will result in fresher
apples. Eventually, new urban markets may also be developed.
Transport Savings
9.        The increase in the orchard-gate value of the apples handled by
HPMC would result not only from their higher wholesale values, but also
from reduced transport costs. Orchard to roadhead transport costs should
be one-third lower with the project than without it, because the new roads
and cableways would reduce porterage distances. Transport costs from the
packing station to the final markets would fall on average by one-fourth
because apples not destined for Delhi could go direct to their destination
without passing through Delhi or other intermediate markets. There would
also be some packing station-to-market transport savings on customer apples
because due to its higher efficiency, HPMC would carry them to Kalka at
below the conventional rate.  And, an estimated 5% of total apple production
which now rots because it cannot get to market in time (due to landslides
which close the roads, the breakdown of trucks on the rough roads, or the
unavailability of porters at certain times), would be saved by the transport
improvements, especially the road maintenance equipment.
10.       Savings on transport would not be restricted to apples handled by
HPMC. The reductions in spoilage and in the orchard to roadhead transport
cost would also benefit other apple growers in the project areas. By 1977,
these growers would market about 60,000 tons on which they would save about
Rs 70 per ton, or Rs 4.2 million in total. To be conservative, output
expansions beyond 1977 are not taken into account.
11.       Savings on non-apple transport are difficult to estimate.  Although
goods other than apples account for about 80% of total tonnage on the roads
to be improved, because the new roads and cableways would be located pri-
marily to serve the orchards, and because prompt, smooth carriage is less
important for most other products, we assume conservatively that the
transport savings of all other users equal half the total transport savings
on apples from the infrastructure improvements (half of Rs 7.8 million, or
Rs 3.9 million per annum at full project development).



ANNEX 14
Page 4
12.       Finally, the prolect roads would induce new agricultural develop-
ment in areas where it is at present held back by high transport costs on
inputs and outcuts. Only rough guesses of these benefits can be made, and
it is assumed that witnin 3 km on both sides of the 400 km of project
roads, 4,000 ha would be cultivated (vs 16,000 ha already under crops, out
of a total area of 240,000 ha), with average gross output of Rs 1,640 per
ha (the present average--see Annex 1, Table 2), of which 25% is net profits.
This yields total annual benefits of Rs 1.6 million.
Summary of Quantifiable Benefits
13.       The benefits would accrue to RPMC, project apple growers, and
other users of the improved transportation system.  The annual economic
benefits of HPMC at full development total Rs 44.1 million, divided as
follows:
(a) sales price of cold store apples (Rs 2,623/ton) minus
purchase price (Rs 1,205/ton) times 10,000 tons =
Rs 14.2 million;
(b) sales price of consignment apples (Rs 2,155/ton) minus
purchase price (Rs 1,305/ton) times 10,000 tons = Rs 8.5
million;
(c) packing fee (Rs 385/ton), roadhead to packing station
haulage charge (Rs 14/ton), and forwarding charge to
Kalka (Rs 135/ton) for customer apples, times 20,000
tons = Rs 10.7 million;
(d) sales revenue from juice concentrate (Rs 8.8 million)
less farmers' income from culls (Rs 2.8 million) =
Rs 6.0 million;
(e) cableway rental fees of Rs 3.8 million;
(f) cold storage fees for other customers of Rs 0.5 million;
(g) interest on short-term deposits of Rs 0.4 million.
14.       To these are added the grower benefits, which are derived in
Annex 14, Table 1. Apples marketed by HPMC would earn growers an average
of Rs 558 per ton more than without the project; those packed and graded
for a fee would benefit the growers by Rs 164 per ton. With 20,000 tons
in eaca category, growers using EPMC would have a total annual income
increase of Rs 14.4 million at full development. Adding this income to
the HPMC revenue involves no double counting because all the costs for
which HPMC charges the growers have been subtracted in arriving at the
growers' total income from the project.  Together the benefits accruing
to kPMC (except for custone-: cold storage and interest) and the growers
using HPMC would equal the difference between the orchard-gate value of



ANNEX 14
Page 5
the apples without the project (i.e., the opportunity cost of the apples)
and the wholesale value of the apple and juice concentrate with the
project (except for those operations not affected by the project and not
involving project costs, such as the orchard-to-cable way or roadhead
transport by porters, and the transport charges and commissions paid on
customer apples after they leave HPMC).
15.       Total project benefits at full development thus would come to
Rs 44.1 million HPMC revenue, Rs 14.4 million to farmers using HPMC,
Rs 4.2 million to other apple growers, Rs 1.6 million development benefits
and Rs 3.9 million of other road-user savings, or a total of Rs 68.2 million.
Nonquantifiable Benefits
16.       The main nonquantifiable benefits are the development of a new
marketing institution which might later be able to handle additional
functions, and the social gains from improving the transportation system
in an isolated part of the country. Should production of new crops in
the area later be proved viable, for example, these nonquantifiable
benefits might prove to have been very great indeed.
D. Rate of Return and Sensitivity
17.       The project's economic rate of return was calculated to be 23%.
Because of the various project components and assumptions involved, a
number of sensitivity tests were carried out. A 25% increase or decrease
in the opportunity cost of foreign exchange would lower or raise the rate
of return by only 2%. Thus, using thy official exchange rate of Rs 7.5 -
$1.00 would yield a 25% rate of return. A change of 100% in the develop-
ment benefits and non-apple road-user savings would alter the rate of
return by just 3%, in either direction. An increase or decrease of 50%
in the benefits to the apple farmers would raise or lower the rate of
return by about 7%. A 10% increase or decrease in the total costs in
Annex 14, Table 2 would lower or raise the rate of return by around 5%.
While it appears unlikely that the economic viability of the project would
be endangered, the relatively high sensitivity to even a small cost increase
means that the management of HPMC (which will incur' 96% of the project costs
after the initial investment) must be highly competent and cost-conscious.
E. Income Transfers
18.       The quantified net benefits would all be gains in economic
efficiency. In addition, there would be income transfers to the HP
apple growers from two wealthier segments of the population: apple
middlemen and urban apple consumers. As a result of competition from
HPMC, the middlemen would probably have to reduce the commissions they



ANNEX 14
Page 6
charge the farmers. In particular, the project would considerably reduce
the market power of some 140 commission agents/wholesalers in Delhi who
control the existing marketing system but are not in a position (even if
they were willing) to invest sufficiently in improved marketing facilities
and to streamline apple marketing to the benefit of the Indian economy.
19.       The consumer-to-producer income transfer would be due to the
increase in the price of all apples during the peak season which would
result from the diversion by HPMC of apples into off-season markets.
This transfer would be partially offset by the concomitant off-season
price reductions, but as the off-season apple trade is much smaller,
the net transfer probably is to the producers. However, as the seasonal
demand curves for apples are not known, the magnitude of the transfer
cannot be determined.
